⏺ Done. The flow-step response now models a configurable ramp rather than
  only an instantaneous step.                                             
                                                                          
  What changed                                                            
                                                                          
  - model.js — flowStepResponse now takes the flow from "from" → "to"     
  linearly over step_ramp_time seconds, recomputing the convection 
  coefficient h at every timestep as the flow ramps (rather than jumping h
  once at t=0). The explicit-solver stability timestep uses the largest h
  over the ramp. step_ramp_time = 0 reproduces the original instantaneous
  step exactly.
  - index.html — a "Flow ramp time (0 = step)" slider (0–60 s) in the
  Flow-step section; default 5 s.                                         
  - app.js — the graph adds a "ramp end" marker, and the caption notes
  whether it was an instantaneous step or ramped, plus the response times.
                                                                          
  ┌────────────┬────────────────┬────────┐                                
  │    Ramp    │ 30→5 l/min t63 │  t90   │                                
  ├────────────┼────────────────┼────────┤                                
  │ 0 s (step) │ 1.6 s          │ 4.1 s  │                                
  ├────────────┼────────────────┼────────┤                                
  │ 5 s        │ 5.3 s          │ 7.9 s  │                                
  ├────────────┼────────────────┼───────┤                                 
  │ 15 s       │ 14.1 s         │ 16.7 s │                                
  └────────────┴────────────────┴────────┘                                
                                                                          
  The final NTC value is unchanged (steady-state is path-independent), but
  the response now shows the characteristic S-curve: the NTC lags through 
  the ramp, then settles after it. The total reaction ≈ ramp duration +   
  the ~few-second thermal lag of the head. Verified in-browser with no    
  console errors.                                                         
                                                                          
  This makes the realism point concrete: if your impeller loses flow      
  gradually over, say, 5 s, the sensor reflects it within ~8 s; if it's a 
  sudden blockage (near-instant), it's within ~4 s. Either way the thermal
  lag itself is small, so the dominant factor in detection latency is how 
  fast the flow actually changes — plus any alarm debounce you add in     
  firmware.

❯ Can you look at what the accuracy of the flow rate will be given that

  temperatures, hence NTC resistance is quite small over the range of     
  flows from 0 to 40l/m.                                                  
                                                                          
⏺ Excellent and important question — this gets at whether the sensor is   
  actually usable for flow measurement, not just whether the model says   
  the temperatures differ. The concern is real: the flow calibration curve
  is steep at low flow but nearly flat at high flow, so combined with a   
  noisy ADC the high-flow resolution could be poor. Let me build the full 
  measurement chain (NTC resistance → divider voltage → ADC noise → flow  
  uncertainty) and graph it.                                              

  First, the model — NTC/divider/ADC parameters and an accuracy sweep:

⏺ Now add the NTC/divider/accuracy functions. Let me place them after

⏺ Update(design/model-explorer/model.js)                                  
  ⎿  Added 60 lines                                                       
      678    }                                                            
      679                                                                 
      680    // ---------------------------------------------------       
           -------------------------                                      
      681 +  // Measurement chain: NTC -> divider -> ADC -> flow ac       
          +curacy                                                  
      682 +  // ---------------------------------------------------
          +-------------------------                               
      683 +                                                        
      684 +  // NTC resistance (ohms) at a temperature, Beta model 
          +referenced to 25°C.                                     
      685 +  function ntcResistance(p, tempC) {                    
      686 +    const T = tempC + 273.15,                           
      687 +      T0 = 298.15;                                      
      688 +    return p.ntc_r25 * 1000 * Math.exp(p.ntc_beta * (1 /
          + T - 1 / T0));                                          
      689 +  }                                                     
      690 +                                                        
      691 +  // Divider output voltage seen by the ADC. NTC on the 
          +high side (to Vsupply),                                 
      692 +  // series resistor to ground, so hotter (lower R) -> h
          +igher voltage.                                          
      693 +  function dividerVoltage(p, rntc) {                    
      694 +    const rs = p.div_rseries * 1000;                    
      695 +    return (p.div_vsupply * rs) / (rs + rntc);          
      696 +  }                                                     
      697 +                                                        
      698 +  // Effective RMS measurement noise at the ADC input (v
          +olts): ADC repeatability                                
      699 +  // reduced by averaging, combined with quantisation.  
      700 +  function adcSigmaVolts(p) {                           
      701 +    const noise = (p.adc_noise_mv / 1000) / Math.sqrt(Ma
          +th.max(1, p.adc_averages));                             
      702 +    const lsb = p.div_vsupply / Math.pow(2, p.adc_bits);
      703 +    return Math.sqrt(noise * noise + (lsb * lsb) / 12); 
      704 +  }                                                     
      705 +                                                        
      706 +  /*                                                    
      707 +   * Flow-rate accuracy. Sweeps flow 0..hiLpm and at eac
          +h point computes the NTC                                
      708 +   * temperature, resistance, divider voltage, the local
          + sensitivity dV/dflow, and                              
      709 +   * the resulting 1σ flow uncertainty σ_lpm = σ_V / |dV
          +/dflow|. Because the                                    
      710 +   * calibration flattens at high flow, σ_lpm grows — th
          +is quantifies how usable                                
      711 +   * the flow reading is across the range for a given pr
          +obe + ADC.                                              
      712 +   */                                                   
      713 +  function flowAccuracy(p, hiLpm = 40) {                
      714 +    const sigmaV = adcSigmaVolts(p);                    
      715 +    const N = 80,                                       
      716 +      d = 0.5; // l/min step for the numerical derivativ
          +e                                                       
      717 +    const tAtFlow = (lpm) =>                            
      718 +      lumpedSteadyState(Object.assign({}, p, { velocity:
          + lpmToVelocity(lpm, p) }), 'flowing').T_ntc;            
      719 +    const vAtFlow = (lpm) => dividerVoltage(p, ntcResist
          +ance(p, tAtFlow(lpm)));                                 
      720 +                                                        
      721 +    const curve = [];                                   
      722 +    for (let i = 0; i <= N; i++) {                      
      723 +      const lpm = (hiLpm * i) / N;                      
      724 +      const T = tAtFlow(lpm);                           
      725 +      const V = vAtFlow(lpm);                           
      726 +      const dVdlpm = (vAtFlow(lpm + d) - V) / d;        
      727 +      const sigma_lpm = Math.abs(dVdlpm) > 1e-12 ? sigma
          +V / Math.abs(dVdlpm) : Infinity;                        
      728 +      curve.push({                                      
      729 +        lpm,                                            
      730 +        T_ntc: T,                                       
      731 +        R: ntcResistance(p, T),                         
      732 +        V,                                              
      733 +        mV: V * 1000,                                   
      734 +        sigma_lpm,                                      
      735 +      });                                               
      736 +    }                                                   
      737 +    return { curve, sigmaV, sigma_mV: sigmaV * 1000 };  
      738 +  }                                                     
      739 +                                                        
      740 +  // ---------------------------------------------------
          +-------------------------                               
      741    // Public API
      742    // ---------------------------------------------------
           -------------------------
      743    global.SensorModel = {

⏺ This is a compelling, quantitative answer to your concern. The numbers  
  confirm exactly what you suspected — with bare 316 stainless the NTC    
  resistance changes only ~410 Ω across 5→40 l/min, so:                   
                                                                          
  ┌──────────┬────────────────┬─────────────────┐                         
  │   Flow   │ Bare 316 (±1σ) │ Al sleeve (±1σ) │                         
  ├──────────┼────────────────┼─────────────────┤                         
  │ 5 l/min  │ ±1.3           │ ±0.3            │                         
  ├──────────┼────────────────┼─────────────────┤
  │ 15 l/min │ ±6.1           │ ±1.1            │
  ├──────────┼────────────────┼─────────────────┤                         
  │ 30 l/min │ ±17            │ ±3.0            │
  ├──────────┼────────────────┼─────────────────┤                         
  │ 40 l/min │ ±26            │ ±4.6            │                         
  └──────────┴────────────────┴─────────────────┘

  (16× averaging, ~5 mV effective noise.) Bare stainless is essentially   
  unusable for flow above ~15 l/min; the conductive variants are ~4–5×
  better. Let me build this into the tool with controls and graphs. First 
  the graph panels:

      378 +  function renderAccuracy() {                                  
      379 +    const hi = 40;                                             
      380 +    const a = M.flowAccuracy(params, hi);               
      381 +    const sig = a.sigma_mV;                             
      382 +                                                        
      383 +    // signal: voltage curve with ±2σ noise band        
      384 +    const signalPts = a.curve.map((c) => ({ x: c.lpm, y:
          + c.mV }));                                              
      385 +    const hiPts = a.curve.map((c) => ({ x: c.lpm, y: c.m
          +V + 2 * sig }));                                        
      386 +    const loPts = a.curve.map((c) => ({ x: c.lpm, y: c.m
          +V - 2 * sig }));                                        
      387 +    const nearest = (lpm) =>                            
      388 +      a.curve.reduce((b, c) => (Math.abs(c.lpm - lpm) < 
          +Math.abs(b.lpm - lpm) ? c : b));                        
      389 +    const cur = nearest(params.flow_lpm);               
      390 +    charts.signal.setData(                              
      391 +      [                                                 
      392 +        { name: 'ADC voltage', color: '#2d7dd2', points:
          + signalPts },                                           
      393 +        { name: '±2σ noise', color: '#e15554', points: h
          +iPts, dashed: true },                                   
      394 +        { name: '', color: '#e15554', points: loPts, das
          +hed: true },                                            
      395 +      ],                                                
      396 +      [],                                               
      397 +      [{ x: cur.lpm, y: cur.mV, label: `${params.flow_lp
          +m.toFixed(0)} l/min`, color: '#33405a' }]               
      398 +    );                                                  
      399 +                                                        
      400 +    // resolution: ±1σ flow uncertainty (clipped to the 
          +chart's 25 l/min ceiling)                               
      401 +    const CLIP = 25;                                    
      402 +    const resPts = a.curve.map((c) => ({ x: c.lpm, y: Ma
          +th.min(c.sigma_lpm, CLIP) }));                          
      403 +    charts.resolution.setData(                          
      404 +      [{ name: '±1σ flow uncertainty', color: '#f26419',
          + points: resPts }],                                     
      405 +      [],                                               
      406 +      [                                                 
      407 +        {                                               
      408 +          x: cur.lpm,                                   
      409 +          y: Math.min(cur.sigma_lpm, CLIP),             
      410 +          label: cur.sigma_lpm > CLIP ? '> ±25' : `±${cu
          +r.sigma_lpm.toFixed(1)}`,                               
      411 +          color: '#33405a',                             
      412 +        },                                              
      413 +      ]                                                 
      414 +    );                                                  
      415 +                                                        
      416 +    const at = (lpm) => {                               
      417 +      const s = nearest(lpm).sigma_lpm;                 
      418 +      return s > CLIP ? '>±25' : '±' + s.toFixed(1);    
      419 +    };                                                  
      420 +    const mV5 = nearest(5).mV,                          
      421 +      mV40 = nearest(40).mV;                            
      422 +    document.getElementById('accInfo').textContent =

  ⎿  Allowed by auto mode classifier                                      
                                                                          
⏺ Done. I modelled the full measurement chain and added two graphs plus
  controls to answer your accuracy question quantitatively.               
                                                                          
  What I built                                                            
                                                                          
  - model.js — the real signal path: ntcResistance (Beta model),          
  dividerVoltage (NTC + series resistor), adcSigmaVolts (raw ADC noise ÷
  √averages, combined with quantisation), and flowAccuracy which sweeps   
  0–40 l/min and computes the local sensitivity dV/dflow and the resulting
  1σ flow uncertainty σ_lpm = σ_V / |dV/dflow|.
  - NTC & ADC controls — R₂₅, Beta, series resistor, supply, ADC raw noise
  (mV), and averaging count.
  - Two graphs — "Sensor signal vs flow" (ADC voltage with ±2σ noise band)
  and "Flow-rate accuracy" (±1σ uncertainty vs flow), with a live caption
  summarising resolution at 5/15/30/40 l/min.

  The answer

  Your intuition is correct — flow-rate accuracy is poor, and it's a
  fundamental signal-size problem, not just ADC noise. With bare 316
  stainless:

  - The NTC resistance changes only ~410 Ω (and the ADC voltage only ~28
  mV) across the entire 5→40 l/min range.
  - Against ~5 mV effective noise (20 mV raw, 16× averaging): ±1.3 l/min
  at 5 l/min, ±6 at 15, ±17 at 30, ±26 at 40.
  - Without averaging (20 mV) it's hopeless above ~5 l/min.

  So bare stainless is fine for flow present/absent and low-flow alarms,
  but cannot meaningfully resolve high flow rates.

  What helps (all explorable in the tool)

  1. Probe conductivity is the biggest lever — phosphor bronze or the
  aluminium sleeve widen the temperature swing ~4–5×, bringing 30 l/min to
  ~±3 l/min.
  2. ADC averaging — noise scales as 1/√N; 16× already assumed, more helps
  further.
  3. Series resistor matched to the flow-region NTC resistance (~12 kΩ
  here) maximises dV/dT.

  A few honest caveats on the model: it assumes the ADC noise is          
  white/Gaussian and reducible by √N averaging — real ESP32 ADCs also have
  DNL/INL and temperature drift that don't average away, so treat these   
  as best-case. It also doesn't yet include NTC self-heating or tolerance,
  or the reference-temperature reading noise (the θ compensation uses a   
  second noisy NTC read). The headline conclusion is robust regardless:   
  resolving high flow needs a bigger temperature signal, which means a    
  high-conductivity probe. If useful I can add an INL/offset floor (a     
  non-averaging noise term) to make the high-flow numbers more            
  conservative.                                                           
                                                                          
✻ Cooked for 6m 30s

  can't remove, so the total uncertainty should asymptote to them rather  
  than to zero. Let me add a non-averaging floor term combined in         
  quadrature with the averageable random noise.

⏺ Done. Added an INL/offset floor to the noise model.
                                                                          
  What changed                                                            
                                                                          
  - model.js — adcSigmaVolts now combines three error sources in          
  quadrature: random repeatability noise (÷√N), quantisation, and a new   
  adc_inl_mv floor (default 5 mV) that is not divided by √N. So the total 
  uncertainty asymptotes to the floor instead of heading to zero with
  averaging.
  - index.html — an "INL/offset floor" control (0–40 mV) in the NTC & ADC
  section.                                                                
  - app.js — the accuracy caption now breaks out the budget, e.g. "7.1 mV 
  = 5.0 mV random (raw 20 ÷ √16) ⊕ 5.0 mV INL/offset floor", and notes    
  that more averaging only helps until the floor dominates.

  Why it matters (verified)

  The floor changes the conclusion about oversampling. With it included,
  averaging hits a wall:

  ┌──────────────┬─────────────────┬──────────────────────────┐
  │ Averages (N) │ Effective noise │ ±1σ @30 l/min (bare 316) │
  ├──────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
  │ 1            │ 20.6 mV         │ ±70                      │
  ├──────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
  │ 16           │ 7.1 mV          │ ±24                      │
  ├──────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
  │ 256          │ 5.2 mV          │ ±18                      │
  ├──────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
  │ 1024         │ 5.0 mV          │ 17                       │
  └──────────────┴─────────────────┴──────────────────────────┘

  It plateaus at the 5 mV floor — so no amount of oversampling gets bare
  316 below ~±17 l/min at 30 l/min (with floor=0 it would keep shrinking
  to ±0.7 mV / sub-l/min). This reinforces the real fix: a
  higher-conductivity probe to get a bigger temperature signal, not more
  averaging.

  One modelling note: I treat INL as an RMS-equivalent floor combined in  
  quadrature, which captures "averaging can't remove it." Strictly, INL is
  deterministic per ADC code, so if you characterise and correct it (a    
  calibration table per device), the floor drops toward your offset/gain
  residual — worth keeping in mind as a mitigation the model represents
  simply by lowering this one number. The ESP32's eFuse calibration
  already removes the bulk of gain/offset, so 5 mV is a reasonable
  post-calibration starting estimate; raise it if you want a worst-case
  uncalibrated view.

      59    grows sharply with flow. With bare 316 stainless and ~5

         +atters. With the **ESP32 12-bit**

      68 +  ~15 l/min (±17 l/min at 30 l/min on bare 316). With an

⏺ Done. Added an ADC preset selector (ESP32 12-bit ↔ ADS1115 16-bit) and
  decoupled the ADC's full-scale range from the divider supply.           
                                                                          
  What changed                                                            
                                                                          
  - model.js — new adc_fsr parameter (the ADC's full-scale range, which   
  sets the LSB) separate from div_vsupply (the divider's supply). An
  ADC_PRESETS table with the ESP32 internal ADC and the ADS1115, exported
  for the UI.
  - index.html — an ADC preset button group in the NTC & ADC section, plus
  an ADC full-scale control; the noise/INL sliders now allow sub-mV
  values.
  - app.js — preset buttons that set bits/FSR/noise/INL/averages and
  update the sliders; the resolution chart now auto-scales (so it's
  readable for both a noisy and a precise ADC); the caption shows the ADC
  bits and FSR.

  The answer: a 16-bit ADС transforms flow accuracy

  The ADС stops being the bottleneck. 1σ flow uncertainty:

  ┌─────────────────────┬───────────┬───────────┬───────────┐
  │     Probe + ADC     │ @15 l/min │ @30 l/min │ @40 l/min │
  ├─────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼───────────┤
  │ Bare 316 + ESP32    │ ±8.7      │ ±24       │ ±37       │
  ├─────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼───────────┤
  │ Bare 316 + ADS1115  │ ±0.3      │ ±0.9      │ ±1.3      │
  ├─────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼───────────┤
  │ Al sleeve + ESP32   │ ±1.6      │ ±4.3      │ ±6.5      │
  ├─────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼───────────┤
  │ Al sleeve + ADS1115 │ ±0.06     │ ±0.15     │ ±0.23     │
  └─────────────────────┴───────────┴───────────┴───────────┘

  So the ADS1115 buys roughly a 20–30× improvement — bare stainless goes
  from unusable above ~15 l/min to ~±1 l/min across the whole range, and
  with the conductive sleeve you'd be ADC-limited at sub-±0.25 l/min. Why:
  the ADS1115's noise (~0.02 mV after a little averaging) and INL floor
  (~0.25 mV) are ~30× below the ESP32's ~7 mV, so the tiny ~28 mV signal
  across 5→40 l/min is now resolvable.

  Important reality check

  These are ADC-chain-limited numbers — the model says "the electronics
  can resolve this." In a real raw-water system the ADS1115 will expose
  the next limits, which this model doesn't include:

  - Thermal/flow noise — turbulence, intake temperature ripple, bubbles,
  and biofouling drift will likely dominate at the sub-°C level the
  ADS1115 can now see.
  - Reference-temperature accuracy — the θ compensation needs the ambient
  read (heater off); its error propagates into flow.                      
  - NTC tolerance, self-heating, and PTC temperature stability.
                                                                          
  So treat the ADS1115 figures as the best achievable given a stable      
  thermal environment — realistically I'd expect a few tenths of a l/min
  to a l/min once physical effects are included, which is still far better
  than the ESP32. The practical recommendation the tool now makes clear:
  a 16-bit external ADC is worth it, and pairs best with a
  high-conductivity probe — together they move flow measurement from
  "rough/unusable at high flow" to genuinely quantitative. If you'd like,
  I can add a lumped "thermal/flow noise" term (an input-referred °C
  fluctuation) so the accuracy graph reflects that real-world floor too.

⏺ I now have a complete understanding of the reference implementation     
  (model-explorer) and the DoubleNTC concept. The DoubleNTC design is a   
  temperature-compensated constant-temperature-difference (CTD) thermal   
  anemometer: an upstream NTC reads the fluid reference temperature, and a
  downstream NTC is self-heated by a feedback loop to hold a fixed ΔT —   
  the electrical power needed to hold that ΔT is the flow signal          
  (King's-law √v behaviour), which avoids the high-flow saturation that   
  limited the single-probe.

⏺ User answered Claude's questions:                                       
  ⎿  · The single-probe app (model-explorer) showed that flow resolution  
     collapsed at high flow. The DoubleNTC's main claim is that           
     power-to-hold-ΔT keeps rising (King's law). Should the new app       
     include a direct, quantitative head-to-head comparison against the   
     single-probe approach? → Yes, built-in comparison                    
     · The design doc lists two construction options: a 316 tube (5mm OD, 
     3mm ID, 1mm wall) or a 50%-exposed glass-bead NTC. Which should the  
     model support? → Both, selectable (Recommended)                      
     · Maintaining ΔT on a heated element in flowing seawater can need    
     significant power (potentially watts). How important is it to surface
     the power-consumption budget as a first-class analysis? → Minor      
     readout only

 Here is Claude's plan:                                                   
╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌╌
 Plan: DoubleNTC Explorer — second single-page model app                  
                                                                          
 Context                                                                  

 design/DoubleNTC.md proposes a second sensor topology to overcome the
 weakness found
 in the first (single heated probe, explored by design/model-explorer):
 the single probe
 discriminates air/still/flow well but measures flow rate poorly, because
 the head→fluid
 temperature difference shrinks as flow rises, so resolution collapses at
 high flow.

 The DoubleNTC approach is a temperature-compensated
 constant-temperature-difference (CTD)
 thermal anemometer:

 - An upstream NTC reads the stream temperature T_fluid (cold reference).
 - A downstream NTC is self-heated by a differential-op-amp feedback loop
 that drives
 current to hold a fixed temperature rise ΔT (e.g. 10 °C) above the
 upstream reading.
 - The electrical power P needed to hold ΔT is the flow signal. By King's
 law
 P ≈ (a + b·√v)·ΔT, so power keeps rising with flow (no saturation) and,
 because ΔT is
 referenced to the measured fluid temperature, the calibration is
 intrinsically
 temperature-compensated.

 The deliverable is a new offline SPA, design/double-ntc-explorer/,
 mirroring the structure
 and visual language of model-explorer, that lets us explore how this
 approach works and
 quantify how much better it performs — including a built-in head-to-head
 comparison against
 the single-probe model.

 Decisions confirmed with the user:
 - Include a built-in quantitative comparison to the single-probe
 approach.
 - Support both element geometries (316 tube and glass bead), selectable.
 - Power consumption is a readout only — no dedicated power-budget chart.

 Approach

 Create a new self-contained folder design/double-ntc-explorer/ with the
 same 6-file layout
 as model-explorer (index.html, styles.css, charts.js, diagram.js,
 model.js,
 app.js) plus a README.md. Runs offline from file://, zero dependencies,
 everything
 recomputes live — identical conventions to the reference app.

 Files to create

 charts.js — copy verbatim from model-explorer/charts.js (generic
 LineChart, no
 sensor-specific logic). Keeps the new app self-contained.

 styles.css — copy from model-explorer/styles.css; reuse the
 grid/panel/control system
 as-is. Minor additions only if a new readout layout needs it.

 model.js — new physics, pure (no DOM), SI units, same
 IIFE→global.DoubleNTCModel
 pattern as model-explorer/model.js. Reuse the proven helpers from the
 first model
 (re-implemented here to stay standalone):
 - pipeArea, lpmToVelocity, velocityToLpm (identical to model-explorer).
 - Seawater property block and forcedConvection (Churchill–Bernstein,
 cylinder-in-crossflow)
 for the tube element.
 - New sphereConvection (Whitaker: Nu = 2 + (0.4·Re^0.5 +
 0.06·Re^(2/3))·Pr^0.4) for the
 bead element.
 - flowConvection blend with a still-water natural-convection floor; air
 case adds the
 linearised radiation term (same as model-explorer).

 Core new functions:
 - wettedArea(p) — tube: π·d·L_active + tip cap; bead: exposed_frac·π·d².
 - parasiticConductance(p) — conduction loss from the heated element into
 its mount/leads
 (tube wall cross-section over a support length, plus a tunable lead term
 G_lead). This is
 the offset a in King's law and sets the air/zero-flow power floor.
 - heatedElementSteady(p, scenario) → { P, h, G_fluid, G_para, I, V,
 R_ntc, T_heated }:
 with ΔT held constant, P = (h·wettedArea + G_para)·ΔT; T_heated =
 T_fluid + ΔT;
 R_ntc = ntcResistance(T_heated); I = √(P/R_ntc), V = I·R_ntc.
 - deriveCalibration(p) — sweep velocity 0..vmax → [{v, lpm, P, I, V}];
 the heart of the
 tool. Also fit/annotate the King's-law a + b·√v form for overlay.
 - heatedElementTransient(p, scenario, mode) — small explicit-Euler
 lumped model of the
 bead/tube tip thermal mass C with conductance G_fluid+G_para: open-loop
 turn-on/step
 thermal time constant τ = C/(G_fluid+G_para). Report the closed-loop
 reaction as
 τ_open / loop_gain (idealised CTD speed-up) with a tunable loop_gain.
 - flowAccuracy(p, hiLpm) — measurement chain. Read heater current via a
 sense resistor
 (V_sense = I·R_sense); reuse the adcSigmaVolts model
 (bits/noise/INL/averaging,
 ADC presets) from model-explorer. Propagate σ_lpm = σ_signal /
 |dSignal/dlpm|. Because
 P ∝ √v, sensitivity falls only as 1/√v — far better than the single
 probe.
 - deriveDetectionModel(p) — air/still/flow power levels and thresholds;
 lpmFromPower(P)
 inverse lookup. Detection here is by power, not temperature.
 - tempCompensationCheck(p) — sweep T_fluid and show the power-vs-flow
 curve barely moves
 (only via fluid-property drift), demonstrating the built-in compensation
 advantage.
 - Single-probe comparison hook: a compact re-implementation of the
 single-probe
 lumpedSteadyState (R_cond + R_conv, T_ntc) sufficient to compute its
 normalised flow
 sensitivity and resolution, so the comparison chart is self-contained
 (no dependency on
 model-explorer/model.js).
 - NTC helpers ntcResistance, and ADC helpers copied from model-explorer.
 - Export DEFAULTS, ELEMENTS (tube/bead presets), ADC_PRESETS,
 cloneDefaults, and all
 the functions above.

 DEFAULTS (new/changed vs model-explorer):
 delta_T: 10, T_fluid: 20, element: 'tube', tube tube_od_mm: 5,
 tube_id_mm: 3, tube_wall_mm: 1, active_len_mm: 10, bead bead_dia_mm:
 2.5, bead_exposed_frac: 0.5,
 support_len_mm, G_lead, loop_gain, r_sense, sensor spacing_mm, plus
 reused
 seawater/pipe/flow/NTC/ADC/numeric blocks.

 diagram.js — new cross-section: pipe bore with flow arrows L→R, the
 upstream NTC
 bead (cold, reference) and downstream heated NTC element (drawn as tube
 or bead per
 selection, filled with a hot→ΔT colour), the heated wake trailing
 downstream (note it must not
 reach the upstream sensor — a directionality caveat). Overlay the op-amp
 / Wheatstone-bridge
 feedback schematic (bridge with the heated NTC arm, diff-amp, drive
 transistor, sense
 resistor) and a readout of ΔT, P, I, h. Same heatColor, HiDPI,
 drawDiagram(canvas, p, result, scenario) signature as the reference.

 app.js — same wiring pattern as model-explorer/app.js: build .ctrl
 slider/number
 controls from markup, scenario buttons (air/still/flowing), element
 buttons (tube/bead,
 analogous to the material buttons), ADC preset buttons, reset, debounced
 resize redraw.
 recompute() renders the diagram, a readout card grid (ΔT, required P, I,
 V, h,
 flow), and these charts.

 Charts (the analysis):
 1. Power vs flow rate (core calibration) — P to hold ΔT in flowing
 water, air/still
 reference bands, current-flow marker; overlay the King's-law a+b·√v fit.
 Monotonic,
 non-saturating.
 2. Required current / drive vs flow — what the op-amp must deliver
 (feasibility).
 3. Detection levels & thresholds — air (mW) / still / flowing power
 bands.
 4. Flow-rate accuracy ±1σ vs flow — power/current-sense noise
 propagated; the headline
 resolution result.
 5. "Why two sensors" comparison (built-in) — normalised flow sensitivity
 and ±1σ
 resolution of CTD-power vs single-probe-temperature on one axis, showing
 the
 single-probe collapse at high flow vs the CTD's graceful 1/√v.
 6. ΔT sweep — how P and resolution scale with chosen ΔT (5/10/20 °C);
 the SNR vs
 power/boiling-risk tradeoff (boiling-margin note in seawater).
 7. Temperature compensation check — power-vs-flow curves at several
 T_fluid values
 nearly overlapping ⇒ intrinsic compensation.
 8. Turn-on / flow-step transient — heated element reaching ΔT and
 tracking a flow step;
 report open-loop τ and idealised closed-loop reaction time; contrast
 with the single
 probe's slower head/block mass.

 Detection & ESP32 output panel — thresholds table (by power) +
 copy-paste C reference:
 classify_power(P) and flow_lpm(P) lookup, with a note that flow is read
 from heater power
 (current-sense ADC), ΔT is held by the analog loop, and T_fluid comes
 from the upstream
 NTC — no heater-off ambient measurement needed (unlike the single
 probe). Mirror the
 deriveTempCompensation/LUT emission style from
 model-explorer/app.js:472-555.

 README.md — companion doc in the same voice as model-explorer/README.md:
 what each
 graph shows, the model equations (King's law,
 Churchill–Bernstein/Whitaker, power balance),
 the performance comparison conclusion, and assumptions/limitations
 (ideal CTD loop, fixed
 seawater properties, fouling/salt drift, flow-direction dependence,
 upstream self-heating
 assumed negligible).

⏺ Done. I built a second self-contained single-page app at                
  design/double-ntc-explorer/ following the model-explorer pattern, and   
  verified it in the browser.                                             
                                                                          
  What it models                                                          
                                                                          
  The DoubleNTC topology is a temperature-compensated                     
  constant-temperature-difference (CTD) thermal anemometer: an upstream   
  NTC reads the stream temperature, and a downstream NTC is self-heated by
  an op-amp loop to hold a fixed ΔT. The electrical power to hold ΔT is   
  the flow signal — P = (h(v)·A_wet + G_para)·ΔT, with h(v) from          
  Churchill–Bernstein (tube) or Whitaker (bead).                          

  Files

  - model.js — pure physics: power balance, both element geometries,
  King's-law calibration, current/voltage drive, turn-on & flow-step
  transients, ADC accuracy, detection, and a compact single-probe
  re-implementation for comparison.
  - diagram.js — pipe cross-section with upstream/downstream sensors,
  thermal wake, and the bridge→op-amp→FET→sense-resistor→ADC feedback
  schematic.
  - index.html + app.js — controls and 9 live charts.
  - README.md, plus charts.js/styles.css copied verbatim.

  Verified findings (browser-confirmed, no console errors bar a favicon
  404)

  The analysis the user asked for came out clearly:

  1. ~20× better flow resolution. At 30 l/min: DoubleNTC ±1.1 l/min vs
  single-probe ±23 l/min on the same ADC. The CTD power signal rises as √v
  and never saturates.
  2. The 5 mm tube is impractical — holding ΔT in fast seawater needs
  ~24.6 W at 34 V, exceeding a 12 V rail (the app flags this in red). The
  glass bead is the workable choice at ~2.2 W / 10.2 V — its ~20× smaller
  wetted area cuts both power and thermal mass.
  3. A self-heated element must be low-resistance (tens of ohms, not ~10
  kΩ) or the drive voltage is absurd — surfaced via the electrical-drive
  readout.
  4. Intrinsic temperature compensation — power-vs-flow curves at 5–30 °C
  nearly coincide; no heater-off ambient read needed (unlike the single   
  probe).
                                                                          
  The element/scenario/ADC toggles, all charts, and the generated ESP32 C 
  reference all render and update live.
                                                                          
  One judgment call worth flagging: I left the 316 tube as the default    
  element (matching the order in DoubleNTC.md), so the app opens showing
  the tube's power-infeasibility warning — it makes the headline finding  
  immediately visible, and switching to the bead shows the fix. Say the
  word if you'd rather it default to the bead.

      217 +  // ===================================================
          +=======================                                 
      218 +  // Detailed op-amp bridge schematic of the constant-ΔT
          + control loop.                                          
      219 +  //                                                    
      220 +  // A Wheatstone bridge: the LEFT divider (R1 + upstrea
          +m NTC) sets the target at                               
      221 +  // node A; the RIGHT divider (downstream heated NTC + 
          +R2) reports the element at                              
      222 +  // node B. A high-gain differential amplifier drives c
          +urrent into the bridge top                              
      223 +  // (Vt) to keep A = B, which pins the heated NTC's res
          +istance — hence its                                     
      224 +  // temperature — a fixed step (ΔT) above the upstream 
          +NTC. The current it must                                
      225 +  // supply (sensed across R_s) is the flow signal.     
      226 +  // ===================================================
          +=======================                                 
      227 +  function drawCircuit(canvas, p, result) {             
      228 +    const ctx = canvas.getContext('2d');                
      229 +    const dpr = window.devicePixelRatio || 1;           
      230 +    const rect = canvas.getBoundingClientRect();        
      231 +    const W = Math.max(640, rect.width),                
      232 +      H = Math.max(360, rect.height);

      234 +    canvas.height = H * dpr;

          +idth.

      407 +  // vertical zigzag resistor between (x,yTop) and (x,yB
          +ot)                                                     
      408 +  function vResistor(ctx, x, yTop, yBot, name, sub, colo
          +r) {                                                    
      409 +    const lead = 10;                                    
      410 +    const zTop = yTop + lead,                           
      411 +      zBot = yBot - lead;                               
      412 +    ctx.strokeStyle = color || '#33405a';               
      413 +    ctx.lineWidth = 1.6;                                
      414 +    ctx.beginPath();                                    
      415 +    ctx.moveTo(x, yTop);                                
      416 +    ctx.lineTo(x, zTop);                                
      417 +    const seg = (zBot - zTop) / 6,                      
      418 +      amp = 6;                                          
      419 +    for (let i = 0; i < 6; i++) {                       
      420 +      const yy = zTop + seg * (i + 0.5);                
      421 +      ctx.lineTo(x + (i % 2 === 0 ? amp : -amp), yy);   
      422 +    }                                                   
      423 +    ctx.lineTo(x, zBot);                                
      424 +    ctx.lineTo(x, yBot);                                
      425 +    ctx.stroke();                                       
      426 +    if (name) {                                         
      427 +      ctx.fillStyle = color || '#33405a';               
      428 +      ctx.font = '600 11px system-ui, sans-serif';      
      429 +      ctx.textAlign = 'left';

          +- 6);                                                   
      432 +      if (sub) {                                        
      433 +        ctx.fillStyle = '#62708a';                      
      434 +        ctx.font = '9px system-ui, sans-serif';         
      435 +        ctx.fillText(sub, x + amp + 6, (zTop + zBot) / 2
          + + 7);                                                  
      436 +      }                                                 
      437 +    }                                                   
      438 +  }                                                     
      439 +  // horizontal zigzag resistor between (xL,y) and (xR,y
          +)                                                       
      440 +  function hResistor(ctx, xL, xR, y, name, sub, color) {
      441 +    const lead = 10;                                    
      442 +    const zL = xL + lead,                               
      443 +      zR = xR - lead;                                   
      444 +    ctx.strokeStyle = color || '#33405a';

      446 +    ctx.beginPath();

      448 +    ctx.lineTo(zL, y);                                  
      449 +    const seg = (zR - zL) / 6,                          
      450 +      amp = 6;                                          
      451 +    for (let i = 0; i < 6; i++) {                       
      452 +      const xx = zL + seg * (i + 0.5);                  
      453 +      ctx.lineTo(xx, y + (i % 2 === 0 ? amp : -amp));   
      454 +    }                                                   
      455 +    ctx.lineTo(zR, y);                                  
      456 +    ctx.lineTo(xR, y);                                  
      457 +    ctx.stroke();

      460 +    ctx.textAlign = 'center';

      465 +      ctx.font = '9px system-ui, sans-serif';           
      466 +      ctx.fillText(sub, (xL + xR) / 2, y - amp - 15);   
      467 +    }                                                   
      468 +  }

      126          <button id="reset" class="reset">Reset to defaul       
           ts</button>                                                    
      127        </section>                                               
      128                                                                 
      129 +      <!-- ============ HOW IT WORKS: op-amp bridge sche       
          +matic ============ -->                                         
      130 +      <section class="panel circuit-panel">                    
      131 +        <h2>How the constant-ΔT control loop works</h2> 
      132 +        <canvas id="circuit"></canvas>                  
      133 +        <ol class="circuit-explain">                    
      134 +          <li>                                          
      135 +            <b>The bridge.</b> Two voltage dividers hang
          + off a shared, driven top node (Vt).                    
      136 +            The <span style="color:#2d7dd2">left</span> 
          +divider is <code>R1</code> over the                     
      137 +            <b>upstream NTC</b> → node <b>A</b> (the <i>
          +target</i>). The                                        
      138 +            <span style="color:#b23">right</span> divide
          +r is the <b>downstream heated NTC</b>                   
      139 +            over <code>R2</code> → node <b>B</b> (the <i
          +>measurement</i>).                                      
      140 +          </li>                                         
      141 +          <li>                                          
      142 +            <b>ΔT is set, not measured.</b> The op-amp o
          +nly ever forces <code>A = B</code>.                     
      143 +            That balance requires <code>R_dn = R1·R2 / R
          +_up</code>: a fixed resistor ratio pins                 
      144 +            the heated NTC's resistance — and an NTC's r
          +esistance maps to its temperature — a                   
      145 +            fixed step above the upstream NTC. That step
          + <i>is</i> ΔT. You choose ΔT by choosing                
      146 +            the <code>R1/R2</code> ratio (the "overheat"
          +).                                                      
      147 +          </li>                                         
      148 +          <li>                                          
      149 +            <b>Why it's temperature-compensated.</b> <co
          +de>R_up</code> floats with the fluid                    
      150 +            temperature, so the target tracks the stream
          + automatically. As the seawater warms or                
      151 +            cools, the held ΔT stays the same — no separ
          +ate ambient calibration needed.                         
      152 +          </li>                                         
      153 +          <li>                                          
      154 +            <b>The feedback.</b> Flow rises → the heated
          + NTC cools → its resistance rises → node                
      155 +            B drops → the op-amp sees <code>A &gt; B</co
          +de> and pushes <i>more</i> current into                 
      156 +            Vt → more I²R heating → the NTC warms back t
          +o the set point → the bridge re-balances.               
      157 +            A fast, self-correcting loop that holds ΔT c
          +onstant at every flow.                                  
      158 +          </li>                                         
      159 +          <li>                                          
      160 +            <b>The flow signal is the power.</b> Holding
          + ΔT against faster flow takes more                      
      161 +            current. Read that current as the voltage ac
          +ross the sense resistor <code>R_s</code>                
      162 +            → ADC; the heater power <code>P = I²·R</code
          +> is the flow reading. More flow ⇒ more                 
      163 +            current ⇒ more power.                       
      164 +          </li>                                         
      165 +          <li>                                          
      166 +            <b>Bonus thermometer.</b> Node A (the upstre
          +am-NTC divider) also gives the fluid                    
      167 +            temperature directly, for display and as the
          + ΔT reference.                                          
      168 +          </li>                                         
      169 +        </ol>                                           
      170 +      </section>                                        
      171 +                                                        
      172        <!-- ============ MIDDLE: graphs ============ -->
      173        <section class="panel graph-panel full">
      174          <h2>Flow calibration — power to hold ΔT vs flow
           rate</h2>

⏺ Done. I added a proper op-amp circuit schematic with a plain-language   
  walkthrough, in a new "How the constant-ΔT control loop works" panel    
  directly under the sensor diagram.                                      

  What it now shows and explains                                          
                                                                          
  The schematic is a real Wheatstone bridge with a differential op-amp:   
  - Left divider — R1 over the upstream NTC → node A (the target).
  - Right divider — the downstream heated NTC over R2 → node B (the
  measurement).
  - The op-amp compares A and B and drives current into the bridge top
  (Vt) through the current-sense resistor R_s, which taps up to the ADC.
  Live values (ΔT, drive current, power, V_sense) sit alongside.

  The 6-step walkthrough answers your three questions directly:

  1. How ΔT is "measured" — it isn't. The op-amp only ever forces A = B,
  and that balance requires R_dn = R1·R2 / R_up. Since an NTC's resistance
  maps to its temperature, that fixed resistor ratio pins the heated NTC
  a fixed step above the upstream NTC — and that step is ΔT. You set ΔT by
  choosing the R1/R2 ratio (the "overheat").
  2. Why it's temperature-compensated — R_up floats with the fluid, so the
  target tracks the stream automatically.
  3. How the feedback works — flow ↑ → heated NTC cools → its resistance
  rises → node B drops → op-amp sees A > B → pushes more current → more
  I²R heating → NTC warms back to set point → bridge re-balances.
  Self-correcting, holds ΔT at every flow.
  4. Where the reading comes from — holding ΔT against faster flow takes
  more current; read it across R_s → P = I²·R is the flow signal.

  I verified it in the browser: the schematic renders cleanly (node A's   
  tap hops over the right arm as a proper no-connect, R_s/ADC no longer
  collide), the explanation lays out in two columns, and there are no     
  console errors. Servers stopped and screenshot artifacts removed.

  One note: I removed the earlier abstract block-chain                    
  (bridge→amp→FET→R_s→ADC) from the main sensor diagram since the new
  detailed schematic replaces it — let me know if you'd rather keep both. 
                                                                          
✻ Crunched for 11m 28s                                                    
                                                                          
❯ just to be clear, we are heating the NTC by passing a current through   
  the NTC as shown in the schematic, rather than having a seperate heated 
  element. The electrical drive section has a heated element resistance   
  set at a static 47R, but the NTC R is variable, so what is that         
  resistance ?                                                            
                                                                          
⏺ Good catch — you've spotted a real inconsistency in the model. Let me   
  answer precisely.                                                       
                                                                          
  What the resistance actually is
                                                                          
  You're right: in the schematic the downstream NTC is the heater —       
  current passes through the NTC itself, there's no separate element. So  
  R_dn is the NTC's own resistance, and an NTC's resistance is            
  temperature-dependent.                                                  

  The key subtlety: the loop holds the NTC at a fixed temperature T_fluid 
  + ΔT (that's the whole point of constant-ΔT). At a fixed temperature the
  resistance is fixed too. So during operation R_dn is essentially        
  constant — which is why a single static number is the right kind of
  model. The resistance is:

  R_dn = R25 · exp( B · (1/T_op − 1/298.15) ),   T_op = T_fluid + ΔT
  (Kelvin)                                                                
                                                                          
  But the static 47 Ω is inconsistent with the 10 kΩ NTC                  
                                                                          
  Here's the problem. The "NTC & ADC" section sets ntc_r25 = 10 kΩ (used  
  for the upstream sensor and the single-probe comparison), but the       
  "Electrical drive" section sets heater_r = 47 Ω independently. Those    
  describe two different NTCs. With the 10 kΩ part, the operating
  resistance is:

  - T_op = 20 + 10 = 30 °C → R_dn = 10k · exp(3950·(1/303.15 − 1/298.15)) 
  ≈ **8.0 kΩ**
  - To dissipate the 2.2 W the bead needs in flowing seawater: V = √(P·R) 
  = √(2.2·8000) ≈ **133 V**, I ≈ 16.6 mA.                                 
                                                                          
  133 V is absurd — that's exactly why you cannot self-heat an ordinary 10
  kΩ NTC to watts. To make self-heating work, the NTC must be             
  low-resistance: R_dn = V²/P = 12²/2.2 ≈ 65 Ω, i.e. an NTC with R25 ≈    
  50–60 Ω. So the heater_r = 47 Ω is implicitly assuming a low-ohm NTC
  (R25 ≈ 58 Ω), and the 10 kΩ value never feeds the heated arm at all.

  So, to directly answer "what is that resistance?": it should be         
  R25_heated · exp(B·(1/T_op − 1/298)) — constant at a fixed operating 
  point because the temperature is held, but currently it's a disconnected
  hand-set 47 Ω rather than something derived from the NTC's R25/Beta.

  The fix I'd recommend

  Make the heated arm self-consistent:
  1. Add a dedicated low R25 for the heated NTC (default ~58 Ω) — separate
  from the 10 kΩ sensing NTC.                                             
  2. Derive R_dn from the Beta model at T_op = T_fluid + ΔT instead of the
  static heater_r. It stays constant across a flow sweep (temperature is  
  held), but updates correctly when you move the ΔT or fluid-temperature  
  sliders — which also makes the accuracy/ΔT-sweep charts more correct    
  (current-sense SNR scales as 1/√R_dn).                                  
  3. Show the live operating resistance and required voltage in the       
  readout, and keep the "exceeds rail" flag.                              
                                                                          
  This also reinforces a genuine design conclusion: a constant-ΔT         
  self-heated NTC has to be a low-ohm part (or a metal-film element bonded
  to the bead), not a standard 10 kΩ thermistor.                          
                                                                          
  Want me to wire that up? I'd replace heater_r with heated_ntc_r25 (+    
  reuse the existing Beta) and compute R_dn live.
                                                                          
✻ Worked for 1m 23s                                                       
                                                                          
❯ Can you check that the ratio R1/R2 remains constant between 5C and 30C  
  for a pair of 10K 3950k NTC thermistor. Looking at a table, the ratio   
  changes as temperature rises.                                           
                                                                          
⏺ Good observation — I checked it, and you're right: the ratio is not     
  constant. Here's the math and what it means for the design.             
                                                                          
  Why it can't be constant                                                
                                                                          
  Thermistor resistance is exponential in 1/T, not in T:                  
                                                                          
  R(T) = R25 · exp( B·(1/T − 1/T25) )
                                                                          
  So for two identical 10 K / 3950 NTCs, the resistance ratio that        
  corresponds to a fixed temperature difference ΔT is:                    
                                                                          
  R_dn / R_up = exp( B·(1/T_dn − 1/T_up) ) = exp( −B·ΔT / (T_up·T_dn) )   
   [T in kelvin]                                                          
                                                                          
  The T_up·T_dn in the denominator is the killer: it grows as the fluid   
  warms, so for a fixed ΔT = 10 °C the required ratio creeps up with      
  temperature:                                                            
                                                                          
  ┌──────────────┬────────────────┬────────────────────┐                  
  │ Fluid (T_up) │ T_dn = T_up+10 │ required R_dn/R_up │
  ├──────────────┼────────────────┼────────────────────┤                  
  │ 5 °C         │ 15 °C          │ 0.611              │                  
  ├──────────────┼────────────────┼────────────────────┤
  │ 15 °C        │ 25 °C          │ 0.631              │
  ├──────────────┼────────────────┼────────────────────┤                  
  │ 20 °C        │ 30 °C          │ 0.641              │
  ├──────────────┼────────────────┼────────────────────┤                  
  │ 25 °C        │ 35 °C          │ 0.651              │                  
  ├──────────────┼────────────────┼────────────────────┤
  │ 30 °C        │ 40 °C          │ 0.660              │
  └──────────────┴────────────────┴────────────────────┘                  
                                                                          
  That's about +8 % from 5 → 30 °C. A bridge made of fixed resistors can  
  only hold a fixed ratio, so it cannot hold ΔT exactly — exactly the     
  drift your table shows.                                                 
                                                                          
  What a fixed bridge actually maintains                                  
                                                                          
  Run it the other way — fix the ratio (calibrate ΔT = 10 °C at 20 °C) and
  see what ΔT it holds:                                                   
                                                                          
  ┌───────┬───────────────┐                                               
  │ Fluid │ maintained ΔT │
  ├───────┼───────────────┤
  │ 5 °C  │ ~9.0 °C       │
  ├───────┼───────────────┤
  │ 20 °C │ 10.0 °C       │                                               
  ├───────┼───────────────┤
  │ 30 °C │ ~10.7 °C      │                                               
  └───────┴───────────────┘                                               

  So a fixed-resistor bridge gives roughly ±10 % ΔT drift over 5–30 °C.   
  Modest and monotonic — but not the "intrinsic, exact" compensation my
  explanation implied. So my "intrinsically temperature compensated" claim
  is overstated and needs softening to first-order.                       

  A second problem: my schematic's topology is wrong                      
                                                                          
  While checking this I found a real bug. In the schematic I drew, the    
  upstream NTC is the bottom of the left arm and the downstream NTC is the
  top of the right arm — on opposite arms. Working through the balance,   
  that holds the product R_up·R_dn = R1·R2 constant, not the ratio.
  Product-constant is far worse:

  - calibrated for ΔT = 10 °C at 20 °C, at 5 °C fluid it would try to hold
  ΔT ≈ 43 °C — the loop would cook the element.
                                                                          
  To get the well-behaved ratio relationship (the ±10 % table above), both
  NTCs must sit in the same position of their arms — both on top (over
  fixed resistors), or both on the bottom. So the schematic needs fixing, 
  not just the wording.                                                   

  Bottom line / options

  The upstream NTC gives good first-order compensation, but the           
  exponential R(T) leaves a residual ΔT drift of ~±10 % over 5–30 °C, and
  an exact constant-ΔT analog bridge isn't possible with plain resistors. 
  Practical choices:                                                      
  1. Accept the drift (and note the flow calibration should be
  characterised against fluid temperature anyway).                        
  2. Digital servo — measure both NTC resistances with the ADC, compute
  T_up and T_dn, and drive power so T_dn − T_up = ΔT exactly. This        
  sidesteps the bridge entirely and is the natural fit for the ESP32 (it  
  ties back to your earlier point — the heated NTC must be a low-ohm part
  you can pass current through, but you no longer rely on a fixed resistor
  ratio).                                                                 
                                                                          
  Want me to (a) fix the schematic so both NTCs are on the same arm side
  (ratio topology), (b) reword the "compensation" claims in the           
  diagram/README/charts to "first-order, ~±10 % residual", and optionally
  (c) add a small "maintained ΔT vs fluid temperature" chart so the       
  residual is visible? I'd also fold in the low-ohm-NTC fix from your
  previous question at the same time.

✻ Churned for 2m 12s                                                      
                                                                          
❯ accept the drift and calibrate the flow to account for that. However,   
  if the temperature difference has been achieved, the output of the      
  OpAmp will be zero and the heater will not be powered up until the      
  temperature drops. Is there an opamp circuit that will result in a      
  constant voltage/power being applied to the heater when the difference  
  is 5C ?                                                                 
                                                                          
⏺ Great question — and it gets at the single most common misconception    
  about feedback amplifiers. The short answer: the bridge already keeps   
  the heater powered continuously; the op-amp output is not zero when     
  balanced. And yes, there's a specific circuit (an integrator / PI
  controller) that makes that behaviour exact and robust.                 
                                                                          
  Why the output isn't zero at balance

  You're picturing the op-amp as a comparator: output ∝ instantaneous     
  error, so error = 0 → output = 0 → heater off. But in this loop the
  op-amp isn't comparing and reporting — its output is the heater drive.  
  It settles at whatever steady value is needed to hold the balance.

  The trick is the huge open-loop gain A_OL (~10⁵–10⁶):

  V_out = A_OL · (V_A − V_B)                                              
                                                                          
  To get a large, steady V_out you only need a vanishingly small error. So
  in steady state:                                                        
                                                                          
  - error (V_A − V_B) = V_out / A_OL → essentially zero but not actually  
  zero
  - V_out (the heater drive) → large and steady, exactly the value that   
  supplies P = (h·A + G)·ΔT                                               
                                                                          
  If the output ever sagged, the element would cool, R_dn would shift, a  
  real error would appear, and the high gain would instantly shove the    
  output back up. The equilibrium is "tiny standing error → full holding  
  drive." It never switches off — it can't, because the fluid is
  continuously carrying heat away and the loop continuously replaces it.

  The everyday analogy: a non-inverting amplifier holds its two inputs    
  equal with a microvolt of error while its output sits at several volts.
  Same thing here.                                                        
                                                                          
  (Note: even my model's proportional controller behaves this way — at    
  steady state it holds a small standing error e = P_hold / K_p, not zero.
  Higher loop gain → smaller standing error, same holding power.)         
                                                                          
  The circuit that makes "hold at zero error" exact: an integrator (PI)   
                                                                          
  If you want the textbook "zero input → constant non-zero output"        
  property explicitly, that is precisely an op-amp integrator, and in     
  practice a PI (proportional + integral) controller:                     
                                                                          
     bridge error e = (V_A − V_B)
          │
          ▼
     ┌───[ R ]──┬─────┐                                                   
     │          │     │            integrator/PI:                         
    (in-amp)    │   [ C_f ]        • e = 0  → output HOLDS (cap keeps its 
  charge)                                                                 
     measures   └─||─┤             • e ≠ 0  → output RAMPS until e → 0    
     A−B          ▼   │            (add R_f ∥ C_f for the proportional    
  term = PI)                                                              
                ┌──────┐                                                  
                │  ─    │                                                 
          ──────┤−   ▶──┼──► drives the base/gate of a                    
                │  +    │      power transistor (BJT/MOSFET)              
                └──────┘            │                                     
                                    ▼                                     
                           supplies the bridge-top current                
                           (the watts the op-amp can't source itself)     
                                    │
                               [ R_sense ] → ADC  (flow signal)           
                                                                          
  The integrating capacitor is the key: with zero error its output stays  
  put (the cap holds its charge), so it delivers a steady, non-zero drive 
  indefinitely — and any small error ramps it back to exactly the right   
  level, giving zero steady-state error. Add a proportional path (PI) for
  fast response without overshoot.

  The power transistor matters too: a small-signal op-amp can't source    
  ~200 mA at ~10 V into the heated NTC, so the op-amp sets the drive level
  and the transistor delivers the actual heating current (this also ties  
  back to your low-ohm-NTC point — the element has to be a few tens of
  ohms for the transistor to push watts at a sane voltage).

  So, to your question directly                                           
                                                                          
  - Once ΔT = 5 °C is reached, the heater is not switched off — it keeps  
  receiving the steady power needed to hold 5 °C against the flow carrying
  heat away. That holding power is the flow signal.                       
  - The only time power genuinely drops to ~0 is if the element is already
  hotter than the setpoint (e.g. right after flow stops) — then the loop  
  coasts until it cools back to ΔT.
  - A PI/integrator stage (plus a pass transistor) is the right circuit to
  guarantee a sustained drive with zero standing error.                   
                                                                          
  My current schematic lumps this into one "power op-amp / driver" box,   
  and the walkthrough's step 4 wording ("pushes more current") describes  
  the transient and could reinforce the zero-at-balance idea.             
                                                                          
  Want me to update the schematic to show the diff-amp → PI/integrator →  
  power transistor → R_sense chain explicitly, and reword the walkthrough
  to say the op-amp holds a steady drive (tiny standing error) rather than
  implying it only reacts to changes?                                     

✻ Brewed for 1m 35s                                                       

❯ Ok  makes sense. I want to seperate the heater element from the NCTs.   
  Power the NCTs with a constant voltage (eg 5V), and use the output of   
  the OpAmp through a suitable driver to power a 40W 12V ceramic heater.  
  Can this be done as a pure analogue circuit ?                           
                                                                          
⏺ Yes — and honestly this is the better architecture. Decoupling the      
  heater from the NTCs removes the awkward constraints we hit earlier (the
  low-ohm self-heated NTC, the 130 V problem) and it can be built         
  entirely in analog. It's also how most real thermal mass-flow meters are
  actually constructed: a dedicated heater with separate, low-power       
  temperature sensors.                                                    

  The all-analog signal chain

   5V ref ─┬─[R]─┬─ R_up (NTC)        ┌──────────┐   ┌─────────────┐
  ┌──────────┐
           │     A (upstream)         │ in-amp / │   │  PI control │   │
  driver  │   12V                                                         
           │                          │ diff amp ├──►│ (op-amp +   ├──►│  
  (PWM or  ├──► [40W heater]                                              
           ├─[R]─┬─ R_dn (NTC)        │  (A−B)   │   │  integrator)│   │  
  linear) │      3.6Ω                                                     
           │     B (downstream)       └──────────┘   └─────────────┘      
  └────┬─────┘                                                            
          GND    bridge sets ΔT ratio                                     
  [R_sense]→ ADC (flow)                                                   
                                                                          
  1. Sensing bridge, 5 V excitation. Both NTCs sit in a low-current       
  divider/bridge — now pure sensors, no self-heating to speak of. With a  
  ~10–47 kΩ series resistor the NTC dissipates well under 1 mW, so        
  self-heating is <0.1 °C in water (worst-case a few tenths in air, where
  we only need to detect "dry" anyway). The fixed resistors set the ΔT
  ratio exactly as before (with the ±10 % drift you've chosen to calibrate
  out).
  2. Difference amplifier (an instrumentation amp, or a one-op-amp diff
  amp) produces the bridge error V_A − V_B.                               
  3. PI / integrator — the stage we discussed: holds a steady output at   
  zero error, ramps to correct otherwise. This is the control voltage.    
  4. Driver to the 40 W heater — this is the only real design choice      
  (below).                                                                
  5. R_sense on the heater return gives the average current → power → the 
  flow reading.                                                           
                                                                          
  The driver: linear vs analog PWM                                        
                                                                          
  A 40 W / 12 V ceramic heater is 3.6 Ω, up to 3.33 A. Two pure-analog    
  ways to drive it:                                                       
                                                                          
  Linear pass device                                                      
  How: PI output drives the gate of a power MOSFET in series with the
    heater                                                                
  Pros: dead simple, no switching noise
  Cons: the FET dissipates (12 − V_heat)·I — easily 5–10 W wasted, needs a
                                                                          
    real heatsink
  ────────────────────────────────────────                                
  Analog PWM (recommended)                                                
  How: a sawtooth oscillator (op-amp relaxation or a 555) + a comparator
    turns the PI voltage into a duty cycle, switching the MOSFET on/off   
  Pros: efficient (FET is fully on or off), no firmware                   
  Cons: a bit more circuitry; switching ripple (filtered by the element's
    thermal mass)

  Both are "pure analog" — the PWM version uses no microcontroller, just a
  comparator and an oscillator. For a standalone marine device, go PWM;
  the heater + element thermal mass averages the pulses, so the NTC never 
  sees the ripple.                                                        

  What this buys you (and the gotchas)

  - It rescues the tube. Earlier the 5 mm tube was infeasible because a   
  self-heated NTC couldn't dissipate ~25 W at a sane voltage. A dedicated
  40 W heater delivers that easily: tube needs ~24.6 W (≈61 % duty, or 9.4
  V/2.6 A linear); the bead needs ~2.2 W (≈5.5 % duty). So 40 W 
  comfortably covers both with headroom — though it's oversized for a
  bead, and you could use a smaller heater there.
  - Heater↔NTC coupling is the new critical detail. The NTC now senses the
  heater through a thermal path, so bond them close (both in a small      
  metal tip / the tube wall) — otherwise you get a steady offset (NTC
  reads cooler than the heater) and added lag that limits loop bandwidth. 
  The loop holds the NTC at ΔT, which is what you want, but only if the
  coupling is tight and repeatable.
  - Two thermal poles (heater mass, sensor/tip mass) plus the integrator →
  tune the PI for stability (a touch of proportional + dominant           
  integrator is the usual recipe).
  - Power budget is real: holding ΔT in fast seawater is genuinely watts  
  (≈2 W bead, ≈25 W tube). PWM keeps the supply draw equal to the         
  delivered power; linear roughly doubles it.
                                                                          
  Offer                                                                   
                                                                          
  This is a meaningful architecture change for the explorer. Want me to:  
  1. Redraw the schematic as this separated chain (5 V sensing bridge →   
  in-amp → PI → PWM/linear driver → 40 W heater → R_sense), and           
  2. Update the model's "Electrical drive" section — replace the
  self-heated heater_r/low-ohm-NTC with a heater_R (3.6 Ω), heater_P_max  
  (40 W) and a duty-cycle readout, drop the "exceeds rail" infeasibility  
  for the tube, and split the NTCs out as low-power sensors?              
                                                                          
  That would make the tool reflect the design you're actually going to    
  build.

      534      ctx.fill();

      536                                                                 
      537 +  function roundRect(ctx, x, y, w, h, r) {                     
      538 +    ctx.beginPath();                                           
      539 +    ctx.moveTo(x + r, y);                               
      540 +    ctx.arcTo(x + w, y, x + w, y + h, r);               
      541 +    ctx.arcTo(x + w, y + h, x, y + h, r);               
      542 +    ctx.arcTo(x, y + h, x, y, r);                       
      543 +    ctx.arcTo(x, y, x + w, y, r);                       
      544 +    ctx.closePath();                                    
      545 +  }                                                     
      546 +  function block(ctx, x, y, w, h, title, sub, fill) {   
      547 +    ctx.fillStyle = fill || '#fff';                     
      548 +    ctx.strokeStyle = '#9aa6bd';                        
      549 +    ctx.lineWidth = 1.2;                                
      550 +    roundRect(ctx, x, y, w, h, 7);                      
      551 +    ctx.fill();                                         
      552 +    ctx.stroke();                                       
      553 +    ctx.fillStyle = '#33405a';                          
      554 +    ctx.textAlign = 'center';                           
      555 +    ctx.textBaseline = 'middle';                        
      556 +    ctx.font = '600 11px system-ui, sans-serif';        
      557 +    ctx.fillText(title, x + w / 2, y + h / 2 - 6);      
      558 +    if (sub) {                                          
      559 +      ctx.fillStyle = '#62708a';                        
      560 +      ctx.font = '9px system-ui, sans-serif';           
      561 +      ctx.fillText(sub, x + w / 2, y + h / 2 + 8);      
      562 +    }                                                   
      563 +  }                                                     
      564 +  // simple n-channel MOSFET (low-side switch), gate lea
          +d entering from the left                                
      565 +  function mosfet(ctx, cx, cy, color) {                 
      566 +    ctx.strokeStyle = color;                            
      567 +    ctx.lineWidth = 1.6;                                
      568 +    vline(ctx, cx, cy - 12, cy + 12, color); // channel 
          +bar                                                     
      569 +    vline(ctx, cx - 6, cy - 9, cy + 9, color); // gate p
          +late                                                    
      570 +    hline(ctx, cx - 26, cx - 6, cy, color); // gate lead
      571 +    vline(ctx, cx, cy - 24, cy - 12, color); // drain le
          +ad                                                      
      572 +    vline(ctx, cx, cy + 12, cy + 24, color); // source l
          +ead                                                     
      573 +    label(ctx, cx + 14, cy + 3, 'M1', '#62708a', '9px');
      574 +  }                                                     
      575 +                                                        
      576    function drawColorbar(ctx, W, H, p) {
      577      const bx = W - 22,
      578        by = 40,
