      143        <section class="panel circuit-panel">
      144          <h2>How the constant-ΔT control loop works</h2>
      145          <canvas id="circuit"></canvas>
      146          <ol class="circuit-explain">
      147            <li>
      148 -            <b>The bridge.</b> Two voltage dividers hang
          - off a shared, driven top node (Vt).                    
      149 -            The <span style="color:#2d7dd2">left</span> 
          -divider is <code>R1</code> over the                     
      150 -            <b>upstream NTC</b> → node <b>A</b> (the <i>
          -target</i>). The                                        
      151 -            <span style="color:#b23">right</span> divide
          -r is the <b>downstream heated NTC</b>                   
      152 -            over <code>R2</code> → node <b>B</b> (the <i
          ->measurement</i>).                                      
      148 +            <b>Two jobs, separated.</b> The NTCs only <i
          +>sense</i> (a low-current 5 V bridge,                   
      149 +            negligible self-heating); a separate <b>40 W
          + ceramic heater</b> on a 12 V rail does                 
      150 +            the heating. Decoupling them removes the awk
          +ward self-heated-NTC constraints.                       
      151            </li>
      152            <li>
      155 -            <b>ΔT is set, not measured.</b> The op-amp o
          -nly ever forces <code>A = B</code>.                     
      156 -            That balance requires <code>R_dn = R1·R2 / R
          -_up</code>: a fixed resistor ratio pins                 
      157 -            the heated NTC's resistance — and an NTC's r
          -esistance maps to its temperature — a                   
      158 -            fixed step above the upstream NTC. That step
          - <i>is</i> ΔT. You choose ΔT by choosing                
      159 -            the <code>R1/R2</code> ratio (the "overheat"
          -).                                                      
      153 +            <b>The sensing bridge.</b> Two dividers off 
          +the 5 V rail: <code>Rs</code> over the                  
      154 +            <b>upstream NTC</b> → node <b>A</b> (the <i>
          +target</i>), and <code>Rs</code> over the               
      155 +            <b>downstream NTC</b> → node <b>B</b> (the <
          +i>measurement</i>). Balance forces a fixed              
      156 +            ratio <code>R_dn/R_up</code>, which — becaus
          +e an NTC's resistance maps to its                       
      157 +            temperature — pins B a step above A. That st
          +ep <i>is</i> ΔT; you set it with the                    
      158 +            resistor ratio. Because <code>R_up</code> fl
          +oats with the fluid, ΔT tracks the stream               
      159 +            (to first order — ~±10 % over 5–30 °C, which
          + we calibrate out).                                     
      160            </li>
      161            <li>
      162 -            <b>Why it's temperature-compensated.</b> <co
          -de>R_up</code> floats with the fluid                    
      163 -            temperature, so the target tracks the stream
          - automatically. As the seawater warms or                
      164 -            cools, the held ΔT stays the same — no separ
          -ate ambient calibration needed.                         
      162 +            <b>The error amplifier holds a steady drive 
          +— it is not a comparator.</b> The in-amp                
      163 +            takes <code>A − B</code> and the <b>PI loop<
          +/b> (integrator) converts it to a control               
      164 +            level. At balance the error is ~0 but the PI
          + output <i>holds</i> (the integrator                    
      165 +            keeps its value), so the heater keeps gettin
          +g exactly the power needed — it does not                
      166 +            switch off when ΔT is reached.              
      167            </li>
      168            <li>
      167 -            <b>The feedback.</b> Flow rises → the heated
          - NTC cools → its resistance rises → node                
      168 -            B drops → the op-amp sees <code>A &gt; B</co
          -de> and pushes <i>more</i> current into                 
      169 -            Vt → more I²R heating → the NTC warms back t
          -o the set point → the bridge re-balances.               
      170 -            A fast, self-correcting loop that holds ΔT c
          -onstant at every flow.                                  
      169 +            <b>The driver delivers the watts.</b> The PI
          + output sets a <b>PWM</b> duty that                     
      170 +            switches the MOSFET <b>M1</b>, feeding the 1
          +2 V heater. (A linear pass-FET also works               
      171 +            but wastes power.) Flow rises → tip cools → 
          +B drops → error → PI ramps the duty up →                
      172 +            more heat → ΔT restored. Self-correcting at 
      173 -            <b>The flow signal is the power.</b> Holding       
          - ΔT against faster flow takes more                             
      174 -            current. Read that current as the voltage ac       
          -ross the sense resistor <code>R_s</code>                       
      175 -            → ADC; the heater power <code>P = I²·R</code       
          -> is the flow reading. More flow ⇒ more                        
      176 -            current ⇒ more power.                              
      175 +            <b>The flow signal is the delivered power.</       
          +b> Read the averaged heater current across                     
      176 +            the sense resistor <code>R_s</code> → ADC; <       
          +code>P = I_avg·V_drive</code> is the flow                      
      177 +            reading (rises with flow, never saturates un       
          +til the heater hits 100 % duty).                               
      178            </li>                                                
      179            <li>                                                 
      179 -            <b>Bonus thermometer.</b> Node A (the upstre       
          -am-NTC divider) also gives the fluid                           
      180 -            temperature directly, for display and as the       
          - ΔT reference.                                                 
      180 +            <b>Bonus thermometer.</b> Node A (the upstre       
          +am NTC) also gives the fluid temperature                       
      181 +            directly, for display and as the ΔT referenc       
          +e.                                                             
      182            </li>

         - temperature rise ΔT** (e.g. 10 °C)

      54                                                                  
      55  1. **No saturation.** Because `P ∝ √v`, the signal keeps        
          climbing with flow — sensitivity                                
      56     degrades only as `1/√v`, never to zero.                      
      57 -2. **Intrinsic temperature compensation.** ΔT is referenc       
         -ed to the *measured* fluid                               
      58 -   temperature and the dominant physics is `h·A·ΔT`, so t
         -he calibration barely moves with                         
      59 -   fluid temperature — no heater-off ambient measurement 
         -is needed (the single probe needs one).                  
      57 +2. **First-order temperature compensation.** ΔT is refere
         +nced to the *measured* fluid                             
      58 +   temperature, so the calibration barely moves with flui
         +d temperature — no heater-off ambient                    
      59 +   measurement is needed (the single probe needs one). It
         + is not *exact*, though: a fixed-resistor                
      60 +   bridge holds a fixed resistance ratio, but a thermisto
         +r's resistance is exponential in 1/T, so                 
      61 +   the maintained ΔT drifts ~±10 % over 5–30 °C. We accep
         +t that drift and calibrate flow against                  
      62 +   fluid temperature.

      100  ### Heated element: tube vs bead
      101  
      101 -A selector switches the downstream element. It is a deci
          -sive design lever:                                      
      102 +A selector switches the downstream element. With the sep
          +arate 40 W heater, both are now                         
      103 +drivable — the choice is about power draw, response time
          + and flow restriction:                                  
      104  
      103 -| Element            | Wetted area | Air   | Still | 30 
          -l/min flow | Drive @ heater_r=47 Ω |                    
      104 -| ------------------ | ----------- | ----- | ----- | ---
          ----------- | --------------------- |                    
      105 -| 316 tube (5 mm)    | large       | ~165 mW | ~1.2 W | 
          -**~24.6 W**   | ~34 V → **exceeds 12 V rail** |         
      106 -| Glass bead (2.5 mm)| small       | ~16 mW  | ~74 mW | 
          -**~2.2 W**    | ~10.2 V, ~217 mA — feasible |           
      105 +| Element            | Wetted area | Air   | Still | 30 
          +l/min flow | Heater @ 40 W / 12 V |                     
      106 +| ------------------ | ----------- | ----- | ----- | ---
          +---------- | -------------------- |                     
      107 +| 316 tube (5 mm)    | large       | ~165 mW | ~1.2 W | 
          +**~24.6 W**   | ~62 % duty, 2.05 A avg |                
      108 +| Glass bead (2.5 mm)| small       | ~16 mW  | ~74 mW | 
          +**~2.2 W**    | ~5.5 % duty, 0.18 A avg |               
      109  
      108 -The big finding: **the 5 mm tube is impractical** — hold
          -ing ΔT in fast seawater needs ~25 W and                 
      109 -a heater voltage well over a 12 V rail (water's convecti
          -on coefficient is huge). The small                      
      110 -**glass bead is the workable choice**: ~2 W at full flow
          - and faster, because its wetted area —                  
      111 -hence the power and the thermal mass — is ~20× smaller. 
          -A self-heated element dissipating watts                 
      112 -also needs a *low* resistance (tens of ohms, or a bonded
          - heater), not the ~10 kΩ of a sensing                   
      113 -NTC, or the drive voltage becomes absurd.               
      110 +The **glass bead** is the efficient choice: ~2 W at full
          + flow, faster (smaller thermal mass),                   
      111 +and barely taxes the heater. The **5 mm tube** needs ~25
          + W in fast seawater — feasible now that a               
      112 +dedicated 40 W heater supplies it (~62 % duty), where th
          +e earlier self-heated-NTC scheme could                  
      113 +not. So the heater wattage should be matched to the elem
          +ent's wetted area and the maximum                       
      114 +flow/ΔT: 40 W suits the tube with headroom and leaves th
          +e bead loafing.

           °C, glass bead, ESP32 ADC)

     ...

      121 -| Temperature compensation         | linear fit + heater
          --off ambient read | intrinsic |                         
      122 +| Temperature compensation         | linear fit + heater
          +-off ambient read | first-order (bridge ratio), ~±10 % |

          -atic temperature compensation, is the

          -ement's power draw in water and the need                
      126 -for a low-resistance heated element and a current-contro

      129  ## The model (see `model.js`)
      130  
      131  - **Power balance:** `P = (h·A_wet + G_para)·ΔT`, with t
           he element held at `T_fluid+ΔT`.
      132  - **Convection:** Churchill–Bernstein (cylinder) for the
            tube, Whitaker (sphere) for the bead,
      133    blended with a still-water natural-convection floor; a
           ir adds a linearised radiation term.
      133 -- **Electrics:** the element at resistance `heater_r` di
          -ssipates `P`, so `I = √(P/R)`,                          
      134 -  `V_heater = I·R`, and the current-sense voltage `V_sen
          -se = I·r_sense` is what the ADC reads.                  
      134 +- **Heater drive:** a separate heater of resistance `hea
          +ter_R` on a `v_drive` rail, `P_max =                    
      135 +  v_drive²/heater_R`. The PWM driver runs at `duty = P/P
          +_max`; the average supply current is                    
      136 +  `I_avg = P/v_drive`, and the current-sense reading `V_
          +sense = I_avg·r_sense` (r_sense is the                  
      137 +  V/A transresistance of the shunt + amp) is what the AD
          +C reads — linear in delivered power.                    
      138 +- **Sensing NTCs:** low-power on a 5 V bridge; `senseSel
          +fHeating` confirms the rise is ~0.05 °C                 
      139 +  (negligible).                                         
      140  - **Accuracy:** ADC noise (random ÷√N ⊕ quantisation ⊕ I
           NL floor) propagated through
      141    `dV_sense/dflow` to a 1σ flow uncertainty — for both t
           his sensor and the single probe, on the
      142    same ADC.
     ...
      146  
      147  ## Assumptions & limitations
      148  
      144 -- The CTD loop is treated as **ideal** (instantaneous, e
          -xact ΔT); real loop dynamics, op-amp                    
      145 -  bandwidth and bridge non-idealities are out of scope (
          -modelled only as a `loop_gain`).                        
      146 -- Seawater properties are **fixed at ~20 °C**; the tempe
          -rature-compensation chart therefore                     
      147 -  shows the *geometric* compensation only — real fluid-p
          -roperty drift adds a few percent.                       
      149 +- The control loop is treated as **ideal** (instantaneou
          +s, exact ΔT); real PI dynamics, op-amp                  
      150 +  bandwidth, PWM ripple and the heater→NTC thermal coupl
          +ing lag are out of scope (modelled only                 
      151 +  as a `loop_gain`). In hardware, bond the heater close 
          +to the downstream NTC so the coupling is                
      152 +  tight and repeatable.                                 
      153 +- **ΔT compensation is first-order, not exact.** A fixed
          +-resistor bridge holds a fixed resistance               
      154 +  ratio, but thermistor R(T) is exponential in 1/T, so t
          +he maintained ΔT drifts ~±10 % over                     
      155 +  5–30 °C. Calibrate flow against fluid temperature.    
      156 +- Seawater properties are **fixed at ~20 °C**; the tempe
          +rature-compensation chart shows the                     
      157 +  *geometric* compensation only — real fluid-property dr
          +ift adds a few percent.                                 
      158  - The upstream NTC is assumed to read `T_fluid` with **n
           egligible self-heating** and to sit
      159    outside the heated element's wake — true only for the
           **design flow direction**; at zero or
      160    reverse flow the heated wake can warm the reference (a
            real limitation).
      161  - **Fouling / salt scaling** changes the wetted `h` and
           would re-scale the calibration; not
      162    modelled.
      153 -- This tool produces the **model, calibration and detect
          -ion coefficients only**. The bridge /                   
      154 -  op-amp design, current drive, ADC sampling, BLE GATT a
          -nd firmware are downstream work.                        
      163 +- This tool produces the **model, calibration and detect
          +ion coefficients only**. The detailed                   
      164 +  analog design (in-amp, PI compensation, PWM stage, MOS
          +FET), ADC sampling, BLE GATT and firmware               
      165 +  are downstream work.

⏺ Both done and verified in the browser (zero console errors). Here's what
  changed.                                                                
                                                                          
  1. The schematic — redrawn for the separated architecture               
                                                                          
  The "How the constant-ΔT control loop works" panel now shows the real   
  signal chain, left to right:                                            

  5 V sensing bridge (Rs + upstream NTC → A, Rs + downstream NTC → B) →   
  instrumentation amp (A−B) → PI loop (∫ integrator) → PWM driver → MOSFET
  M1 → 40 W / 12 V ceramic heater → R_s current sense → ADC. A dashed     
  "thermal coupling" arrow shows the heater warming the tip the downstream
  NTC sits in, and the live duty %, heater power and V_sense update with
  the controls.

  The 6-step walkthrough was rewritten to match, and now also bakes in the
  two things you raised earlier: the NTCs are drawn as both-bottom arms
  (so balance holds the ratio R_dn/R_up, the well-behaved topology), and  
  step 3 explicitly says the PI integrator holds a steady drive — it's not
  a comparator that switches off at balance.
  (12 V) heater, P_max = 40 W. The driver model is PWM: duty = P/P_max,   
  I_avg = P/v_drive, V_sense = I_avg·r_sense (r_sense is now a V/A        
  current-sense gain). The "exceeds rail" infeasibility is replaced by a  
  duty-cycle / saturation check.                                          
  - The NTCs are now low-power sensors on a 5 V bridge; a new             
  senseSelfHeating() confirms the rise is ~0.05 °C (negligible), surfaced 
  via new "Sensing NTCs (5 V)" controls.                                  
  - The "Drive current & voltage" chart became "Heater duty cycle vs flow"
  with a 100 % saturation band; the readout cards now show power / duty / 
  avg current; and the ESP32 C reference computes P = I_avg·V_drive.      
                                                                          
  Key result this unlocks: the separate 40 W heater makes the 5 mm tube   
  feasible (≈24.6 W = 62 % duty, 2.05 A avg) where the old self-heated    
  scheme couldn't drive it — while the bead loafs at ~5.5 % duty. The     
  README's element table, model section and assumptions were updated      
  accordingly, including the honest "first-order, ~±10 % ΔT drift" note   
  from the earlier discussion.                                            
                                                                          
  One thing I kept the tube as the default element (matches DoubleNTC.md);
  with the new heater it no longer shows a red warning, so that's fine —  
  but say the word if you'd prefer the bead as default.

      216 +  function hatchedRect(ctx, x, y, w, h, fill, line) {          
      217 +    ctx.fillStyle = fill;                                      
      218 +    ctx.fillRect(x, y, w, h);                           
      219 +    ctx.save();                                         
      220 +    ctx.beginPath();                                    
      221 +    ctx.rect(x, y, w, h);                               
      222 +    ctx.clip();                                         
      223 +    ctx.strokeStyle = line;                             
      224 +    ctx.lineWidth = 1;                                  
      225 +    for (let xx = x - h; xx < x + w; xx += 7) {         
      226 +      ctx.beginPath();                                  
      227 +      ctx.moveTo(xx, y + h);                            
      228 +      ctx.lineTo(xx + h, y);                            
      229 +      ctx.stroke();                                     
      230 +    }                                                   
      231 +    ctx.restore();                                      
      232 +    ctx.strokeStyle = line;                             
      233 +    ctx.strokeRect(x, y, w, h);                         
      234 +  }                                                     
      235 +                                                        
      236 +  // Single-NTC probe structure: head wetted in the flow
          + (NTC inside), a

          +r in a PTFE block below the

          +, so a hot gradient runs up

          +d.                                                      
      240 +  function drawProbe(ctx, p, result, xc, pipeMidY, pipeT
          +op, pipeBot, H) {                                       
      241 +    const s = Math.min(                                 
      242 +      5,                                                
      243 +      Math.max(2.5, (H - 56 - pipeMidY) / (p.probe_shaft
          +_len_mm + p.heater_len_mm + p.probe_head_thick_mm))     
      244 +    );

          + 2) * s);

          +);                                                      
      247 +    const shaftHalf = Math.max(3, (p.probe_shaft_od_mm /
          + 2) * s);                                               
      248 +    const heaterHalf = Math.max(7, (p.heater_dia_mm / 2)
          + * s);

          +2) * s);

      252 +    const headBot = pipeMidY + headH / 2;               
      253 +    const shaftTop = headBot;                           
      254 +    const shaftBot = shaftTop + p.probe_shaft_len_mm * s
          +;                                                       
      255 +    const heaterTop = shaftBot;                         
      256 +    const heaterBot = heaterTop + p.heater_len_mm * s;  
      257 +

          +und the shaft (hatched).                                
      259 +    const blockHalf = heaterHalf + 13;

          +bb');

          +aftHalf + 7) * 2, shaftBot - shaftTop, '#ece9e3', '#cfc8
          +bb');                                                   
      262 +                                                        
      263 +    // shaft metal, gradient warm (head end) -> hot (hea
          +ter end)

⏺ The physics confirms the key finding — the probe structure reintroduces 
  the conduction bottleneck:                                              
  - Flowing 30 l/min: needs 21.5 W, heater would have to reach 1451 °C →  
  infeasible (heat_ok false).                                             
  - Even at 5 l/min: needs 568 °C. Still water (1.2 W, 111 °C) and air are
  fine.                                                                   
  - Phosphor-bronze shaft drops R_cond 66→13 K/W and heater temp to 314 °C
  — much closer but still over a 250 °C limit.

       97    (`P = I_avg·V_drive` from the averaged current-sense v       
           oltage), `classify()` by power, and a                          
       98    `flow_lpm(P)` lookup table.                                  
       99                                                                 
      100 -### Heated element: tube vs bead                               
      100 +### Heated element: probe vs tube vs bead                      
      101                                                                 
      102 -A selector switches the downstream element. With the sep
          -arate 40 W heater, both are now                         
      103 -drivable — the choice is about power draw, response time
          - and flow restriction:                                  
      102 +A selector switches the downstream element:             
      103  
      105 -| Element            | Wetted area | Air   | Still | 30 
          -l/min flow | Heater @ 40 W / 12 V |                     
      106 -| ------------------ | ----------- | ----- | ----- | ---
          ----------- | -------------------- |                     
      107 -| 316 tube (5 mm)    | large       | ~165 mW | ~1.2 W | 
          -**~24.6 W**   | ~62 % duty, 2.05 A avg |                
      108 -| Glass bead (2.5 mm)| small       | ~16 mW  | ~74 mW | 
          -**~2.2 W**    | ~5.5 % duty, 0.18 A avg |               
      104 +- **Probe (head + shaft)** — the *same structure as the 
          +single-NTC probe*: a PTFE-sleeved 316                   
      105 +  shaft with only the Ø9.2 head wetted, the NTC in the h
          +ead, and a **Ø6 × 20 mm cartridge heater                
      106 +  in a PTFE block** (dry) at the shaft base. Heat reache
          +s the head only through the shaft.                      
      107 +- **316 tube** — a 5 mm tube whose whole active length i
          +s wetted and heated directly.                           
      108 +- **Glass bead** — a small wetted sphere, heater bonded 
          +directly to it.                                         
      109  
      110 -The **glass bead** is the efficient choice: ~2 W at full
          - flow, faster (smaller thermal mass),                   
      111 -and barely taxes the heater. The **5 mm tube** needs ~25
          - W in fast seawater — feasible now that a               
      112 -dedicated 40 W heater supplies it (~62 % duty), where th
          -e earlier self-heated-NTC scheme could                  
      113 -not. So the heater wattage should be matched to the elem
          -ent's wetted area and the maximum                       
      114 -flow/ΔT: 40 W suits the tube with headroom and leaves th
          -e bead loafing.                                         
      110 +| Element            | Air   | Still | 30 l/min flow | H
          +eater temp @ 30 l/min (ΔT 10 °C) |                      
      111 +| ------------------ | ----- | ----- | ------------- | -
          +-------------------------------- |                      
      112 +| Probe (316 shaft)  | ~40 mW | ~1.2 W | **~21.5 W**  | 
          +**~1450 °C → cannot hold ΔT**     |                     
      113 +| 316 tube (5 mm)    | ~165 mW | ~1.2 W | ~24.6 W      |
          + n/a (heated directly), ~62 % duty |                    
      114 +| Glass bead (2.5 mm)| ~16 mW  | ~74 mW | ~2.2 W       |
          + n/a (heated directly), ~5.5 % duty |                   
      115  
      116 +**The big finding for the probe structure:** putting the
          + heater at the end of a thin 316 shaft                  
      117 +re-introduces the *conduction bottleneck* that limited t
          +he single probe. To hold the head at                    
      118 +ΔT = 10 °C in flowing seawater needs ~21 W delivered *th
          +rough* a ~66 K/W shaft, so the heater                   
      119 +would have to reach **~1450 °C** — impossible. It alread
          +y needs ~570 °C at 5 l/min. The probe                   
      120 +only works in **air and still water**; in flow it satura
          +tes (the head can't be held at ΔT) and                  
      121 +degrades to single-probe behaviour. Levers the tool expo
          +ses: a **high-conductivity shaft**                      
      122 +(phosphor bronze k≈75 drops R_cond 66→13 K/W and the hea
          +ter temp to ~310 °C; copper further), a                 
      123 +**shorter shaft**, a smaller ΔT, or moving the heater ri
          +ght up against the head. The **bead**                   
      124 +avoids the bottleneck entirely (heater bonded directly) 
          +and is the efficient performer; the                     
      125 +**tube** works because it is heated along its wetted len
          +gth rather than through a remote shaft.                 
      126 +

           °C, glass bead, ESP32 ADC)
      128  
      129  | Quantity                         | Single probe | Doub
           leNTC (bead) |

⏺ Done — verified in the browser (no real console errors). The downstream 
  sensor is now the single-NTC probe structure with the separate cartridge
  heater.                                                                 
                                                                          
  What changed                                                            
                                                                          
  New probe element (now the default) — geometrically identical to the
  single-NTC probe:
  - Ø9.2 × 3.4 mm head, the only wetted part, with the NTC inside (2 mm
  from the face);                                                         
  - a PTFE-sleeved 316 shaft (6/3 mm, 21 mm);
  - a Ø6 × 20 mm cartridge heater in a PTFE block (dry, not wetted) bonded
  at the shaft base.
                                                                          
  The diagram panel now draws this in full: head in the flow, the sleeved
  shaft crossing the pipe-wall seal with a hot gradient running up it, and
  the heater in its hatched PTFE block below, labelled with R_cond and
  the live heater temperature. Tube and bead remain as selectable
  alternatives.

  Model: heat is delivered head ← shaft ← heater, so the steady power is  
  still P = (h·A_head + G_lead)·ΔT, but I added the shaft conduction
  R_cond and the heater temperature T_heater = T_element + P·R_cond, plus 
  a feasibility flag against a heater_tmax (default 250 °C). New controls
  cover the probe geometry, shaft conductivity, and the cartridge
  dimensions/max-temp. Turn-on now uses the whole-assembly mass (head +
  shaft + heater); flow-step uses the head mass.

  The headline finding this surfaces

  Putting the heater at the end of a thin 316 shaft re-introduces exactly
  the conduction bottleneck that crippled the single probe:

  ┌────────────────┬────────────────────┬────────────────┬───────────┐
  │    Scenario    │   Power to hold    │  Heater temp    │ Feasible? │
  │                │      ΔT=10 °C      │     needed      │           │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────┼─────────────────┼───────────┤
  │ Air            │ ~40 mW             │ ~33 °C          │ ✓         │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────┼─────────────────┼───────────┤
  │ Still seawater │ ~1.2 W             │ ~111 °C         │ ✓         │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────┼─────────────────┼───────────┤
  │ Flowing 5      │ ~8 W               │ ~570 °C         │ ✗         │
  │ l/min          │                    │                 │           │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────┼─────────────────┼───────────┤
  │ Flowing 30     │ ~21.5 W            │ ~1450 °C        │ ✗         │
  │ l/min          │                    │                 │           │
  └────────────────┴────────────────────┴─────────────────┴───────────┘

  To push ~21 W through a ~66 K/W stainless shaft the heater would have to
  glow at ~1450 °C — impossible. So the probe structure only holds ΔT in
  air and still water; in flow it saturates and degrades to single-probe
  behaviour. The tool flags this in the readout ("Heater temp ⚠ 1451 °C")
  and on the diagram ("can't hold ΔT here — heater would need 1451 °C").

  Levers the tool now lets you explore: a high-conductivity shaft         
  (phosphor bronze drops R_cond 66→13 K/W and the heater to ~310 °C;
  copper further), a shorter shaft, a smaller ΔT, or moving the heater up 
  against the head. The bead avoids the bottleneck entirely (heater bonded
  directly) and the tube works because it's heated along its wetted       
  length — both stay feasible.                                            
                                                                          
  So I implemented the structure you specified faithfully, and it's       
  revealed that this particular arrangement (remote heater + thin         
  stainless shaft) won't sustain constant-ΔT in flowing water. If the     
  intent was for the heater to be closely thermally coupled to the head   
  (short/high-k path), set the shaft short or raise the shaft conductivity
  and the heater temperature drops into a feasible range — want me to     
  change the probe defaults toward that (e.g. bronze shaft, shorter       
  length), or leave stainless to keep the bottleneck visible?             
                                                                          
✻ Baked for 16m 28s

⏺ Done — verified in the browser (only the favicon 404). The diagram now  
  matches your spec.                                                      
                                                                          
  What changed                                                            
                                                                          
  Both NTCs are wall-mounted, not centered:                               
  - The upstream reference NTC is a bead seated on the pipe wall,
  protruding only slightly into the flow, with its lead passing out
  through the wall.
  - The downstream probe seats at the wall too, with only the 3.4 mm head
  poking into the flow (the same exposure as the single-NTC model). The
  PTFE-sleeved 316 shaft and the Ø6 × 20 mm cartridge heater (in its PTFE
  block) now sit entirely behind the wall, dry — no part of the shaft is
  in the flow. A hot gradient runs up the shaft from the heater to the
  head, and the seal flange is drawn at the wall.

  The spacing dimension is drawn up in the flow between the two sensor
  heads, and the heated wake trails downstream along the wall.

  No model change was needed — the wetted area is still the head only     
  (side wall + face + back ring), exactly as the single-NTC model defines
  it, so the physics is unchanged. The earlier finding therefore still    
  stands and is shown on the diagram: holding ΔT = 10 °C at 30 l/min needs
  ~21.5 W pushed through the ~66 K/W shaft, so the heater would have to
  reach ~1450 °C — flagged as "can't hold ΔT here." The probe holds ΔT
  only in air and still water unless you raise the shaft conductivity /
  shorten it (the controls let you).

  If you'd also like the upstream NTC drawn as a small head-on-stub       
  (matching the probe head, since it's the same NTC bead in a similar wall
  fitting) rather than a bare bead, I can make that match too — just say  
  so.                                                                     

✻ Cogitated for 5m 21s

  aliminium sensor is 3mm from the head.

          -**~1450 °C → cannot hold ΔT**     |                     
      116 +| Probe (Al block)   | ~40 mW | ~1.0 W | **~17.5 W**  |

      119  
      119 -**The big finding for the probe structure:** putting the
          - heater at the end of a thin 316 shaft                  
      120 -re-introduces the *conduction bottleneck* that limited t
          -he single probe. To hold the head at                    
      121 -ΔT = 10 °C in flowing seawater needs ~21 W delivered *th
          -rough* a ~66 K/W shaft, so the heater                   
      122 -would have to reach **~1450 °C** — impossible. It alread
          -y needs ~570 °C at 5 l/min. The probe                   
      123 -only works in **air and still water**; in flow it satura
          -tes (the head can't be held at ΔT) and                  
      124 -degrades to single-probe behaviour. Levers the tool expo
          -ses: a **high-conductivity shaft**                      
      125 -(phosphor bronze k≈75 drops R_cond 66→13 K/W and the hea
          -ter temp to ~310 °C; copper further), a                 
      126 -**shorter shaft**, a smaller ΔT, or moving the heater ri
          -ght up against the head. The **bead**                   
      127 -avoids the bottleneck entirely (heater bonded directly) 
          -and is the efficient performer; the                     
      128 -**tube** works because it is heated along its wetted len
          -gth rather than through a remote shaft.                 
      120 +**Why the aluminium block matters:** an earlier revision
          + put the heater at the end of a long thin               
      121 +316 shaft, which re-introduced the *conduction bottlenec
          +k* that crippled the single probe — to                  
      122 +hold the head at ΔT = 10 °C in fast seawater needed ~21 
          +W through a ~66 K/W shaft, demanding an                 
      123 +impossible **~1450 °C** heater. Replacing the shaft with
          + a **near-isothermal aluminium block only               
      124 +3 mm from the head** collapses the path resistance to **
          +R_cond ≈ 3 K/W**, so the heater now runs                
      125 +at just **~83 °C at 30 l/min** — comfortably feasible ac
          +ross the whole flow range. The neck length              
      126 +and conductivity (and the block) are editable, so you ca
          +n see how `R_cond = gap / (k · A_head)`                 
      127 +sets the required heater temperature. The **bead** is st
          +ill the most power-efficient option; the                
      128 +**probe** is the chosen mechanical design and is now via
          +ble thanks to the heat-spreader block.

⏺ Done — verified in the browser (only the favicon 404). Both changes are 
  in, and this revision turns the probe into a feasible design.           
                                                                          
  What changed                                                            
                                                                          
  Both NTCs now use the same housing, drawn identically in the diagram:   
  - Only the Ø9.2 × 3.4 mm head protrudes into the flow (NTC inside),     
  wall-mounted.                                                           
  - A short 3 mm metal neck connects the head to a Ø15 × 5 mm aluminium 
  block behind the wall.                                                  
  - The block has two Ø6 holes: one holds the cartridge heater, the NTC
  leads pass through the other.                                           
  - The upstream reference is the identical housing with its heater
  unpowered (drawn cold/grey); the downstream one is heated (block drawn
  hot).                                                                   
                                                                          
  Model: the aluminium block is treated as a near-isothermal heat
  spreader, so the heater→head path is just the 3 mm neck: R_cond = gap / 
  (k · A_head). The block + heater + neck masses feed the turn-on
  transient; wetted area is the wall-mounted head (front face + the 3.4 mm
  side). New controls cover the neck (length/conductivity) and the block
  (diameter/thickness/hole size); the old shaft and cartridge-dimension   
  controls are gone.

  The key result — the bottleneck is fixed

  Putting the heater in an aluminium block 3 mm from the head collapses
  the conduction resistance:

  ┌───────────────────┬───────────────────┬───────────────────────────┐
  │                   │  Old (21 mm 316   │   New (3 mm neck + Al     │
  │                   │      shaft)       │          block)           │
  ├───────────────────┼┼──────────────────┼─┼────────────────────────┤
  │ R_cond            │ ~66 K/W           │ ~3 K/W                    │
  ├───────────────────┼───────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ Heater temp @ 30  │ ~1450 °C ❌       │ ~83 °C ✓                  │
  │ l/min             │                   │                           │
  ├───────────────────┼───────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ Feasible in flow? │ No (air/still     │ Yes, across the range     │
  │                   │ only)             │ (~44 % duty)              │   
  └───────────────────┴───────────────────┴───────────────────────────┘
                                                                          
  So the design you specified works: at 30 l/min the loop holds ΔT = 10 °C
  with the heater at only ~83 °C and ~17.5 W (44 % of the 40 W heater),   
  with no feasibility warning. Power needs are now ~40 mW (air) / ~1 W    
  (still) / ~17.5 W (30 l/min flow). (The 30 l/min power dropped from
  ~21.5 to ~17.5 W because, with the head wall-mounted, only its front    
  face and protruding side are wetted — the back shoulder is now against
  the wall.)

  The README's element table and diagram description were updated to      
  reflect the aluminium-block housing and the "now feasible" outcome.
                                                                          
✻ Crunched for 11m 15s                                                    
                                                                          
❯ If the sides of the head were embedded in the PTFE not in contact with  
  the water and the aliminium block was thermally connected to the back   
  surface of the head what woudl be the power behaviour ?                 
                                                                          
⏺ Good thought experiment — and it changes the behaviour qualitatively,   
  not just quantitatively. Let me work through it.                        
                                                                          
  The new heat path                                                       
                                                                          
  With the sides in PTFE and the block bonded to the back face, the head  
  becomes a 1‑D conduction stack, sides adiabatic:                        
                                                                          
  Al block (≈T_heater) ── back face ──[head metal]── NTC (2mm from front) 
  ──[head metal]── front face ──► water                                   
                                                                          
  All the heat now leaves through only the front face (area A_f = π/4·d² =
  66 mm², vs the old front+side 165 mm²), and it must pass through the 2  
  mm of head between the NTC and that face. Holding the NTC at ΔT gives:  
                                                                          
  Q = ΔT / ( R_conv + R_fn )
     R_conv = 1/(h·A_f)              (front-face convection)              
     R_fn   = ntc_depth/(k·A_head)   (NTC → front-face conduction, in     
  series)                                                                 
                                                                          
  With 316 (k=15), R_fn = 0.002/(15·66e‑6) ≈ 2.0 K/W.                     
                                                                          
  Two consequences                                                        
                                                                          
  1. Much lower power, and a hard ceiling. As flow rises, R_conv → 0 but  
  R_fn stays, so the power saturates:                                     
                                                                          
  Q_max = ΔT / R_fn = ΔT·k·A_head / ntc_depth ≈ 10·15·66e‑6 / 0.002 ≈ 5.0 
  W                                                                       
                                                                          
  ┌─────────┬────────┬─────────────────┬──────────────────────────────┐   
  │  Flow   │ h (≈)  │  Q now (sides   │ Q proposed (front only, NTC  │   
  │         │        │     wetted)     │          2 mm back)          │   
  ├─────────┼────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┤   
  │ Still   │ 600    │ ~1.0 W          │ ~0.37 W                      │
  ├─────────┼────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┤   
  │ 5 l/min │ ~4000  │ ~6.6 W          │ ~1.7 W                       │   
  ├─────────┼────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┤   
  │ 15      │ ~7500  │ ~12.4 W         │ ~2.5 W                       │
  │ l/min   │        │                 │                              │   
  ├─────────┼────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┤
  │ 30      │ ~10600 │ ~17.5 W         │ ~2.9 W                       │
  │ l/min   │        │                 │                              │   
  ├─────────┼────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┤
  │ 60      │ ~15000 │ ~24.7 W         │ ~3.3 W                       │   
  │ l/min   │        │                 │                              │
  ├─────────┼────────┼─────────────────┼─────────────────────────────┘┤
  │ v → ∞   │ —      │ keeps rising    │ ceiling ~5.0 W               │
  │         │        │ (∝√v)           │                              │   
  └─────────┴────────┴─────────────────┴──────────────────────────────┘
                                                                          
  The "knee" is where R_conv ≈ R_fn, i.e. ~15 l/min. Above that the curve 
  flattens hard — dQ/dflow collapses (≈0.03 W per l/min at 30, ≈0.013 at
  60).                                                                    
                                                                          
  2. The heater runs cold and is trivially feasible. Power is small and   
  the block sits right on the back face, so T_heater = ΔT + Q·(1.4 mm 
  neck) ≈ 30 + 2.9·1.4 ≈ **34 °C** — no feasibility issue at all (vs 83 °C
  now, 1450 °C with the long shaft).                                      

  What it means                                                           
                                                                          
  This is a real trade. Insulating the sides + bonding the block to the   
  back makes the device low-power, low-temperature, easy to drive — but   
  burying the NTC 2 mm behind the only wetted face re-introduces the      
  single-probe saturation: at the flows you care about (15–30+ l/min) the
  power signal flattens, so flow-rate resolution gets worse even though
  air/still/flow detection still separates fine (~0.04 / 0.37 / 2.9 W).

  The dominant lever is the NTC depth: the ceiling is ΔT·k·A/ntc_depth.   
  Put the NTC right at the wetted front face (depth → 0) and the cap
  disappears (Q → ΔT·h·A_f, no saturation, just the smaller front-only    
  area). So the geometry only pays off if the NTC is essentially at the
  wetted surface; 2 mm back is enough to dominate at high flow.

  It's the opposite end of the spectrum from the bead (fully wetted, NTC  
  at the surface → no conduction cap, best resolution).
                                                                          
  Want me to add this as a model option — a "head sides insulated" toggle 
  that switches the probe to front-face-only wetting plus the R_fn = 
  depth/(k·A) series term — so you can see the saturation on the          
  calibration and ±1σ-resolution charts and sweep the NTC depth? It's a
  clean addition to the probe path.

      386 +  function ptfeHatch(ctx, x, y, w, h) {                        
      387 +    ctx.fillStyle = '#ece9e3';                                 
      388 +    ctx.fillRect(x, y, w, h);                           
      389 +    ctx.save();                                         
      390 +    ctx.beginPath();                                    
      391 +    ctx.rect(x, y, w, h);                               
      392 +    ctx.clip();                                         
      393 +    ctx.strokeStyle = '#cfc8bb';                        
      394 +    ctx.lineWidth = 1;                                  
      395 +    for (let xx = x - h; xx < x + w; xx += 5) {         
      396 +      ctx.beginPath();                                  
      397 +      ctx.moveTo(xx, y + h);                            
      398 +      ctx.lineTo(xx + h, y);                            
      399 +      ctx.stroke();                                     
      400 +    }                                                   
      401 +    ctx.restore();                                      
      402 +    ctx.strokeStyle = '#cfc8bb';                        
      403 +    ctx.strokeRect(x, y, w, h);

  ⎿  cleaned

⏺ Done — verified in the browser (only the favicon 404). There's now a    
  Head wetting toggle in the Probe controls: Front + sides (default) vs   
  Front only (sides in PTFE).                                             
                                                                          
  What the toggle does                                                    
                                                                          
  When you select front-only:
  - Wetted area drops to just the front face (the side wall is now in
  PTFE).                                                                  
  - The block is treated as bonded to the back face, and the buried NTC
  adds a series conduction resistance R_fn = ntc_depth/(k·A_head) between
  it and the only wetted surface. The model puts this in series with      
  convection: P = ΔT / (R_conv + R_fn) + parasitics.                      
                                                                          
  The diagram redraws with PTFE collars on the head sides (only the top   
  face exposed) and the block bonded directly to the back; the readout,   
  power curve, duty, accuracy and "why two sensors" charts all update
  live.                                                                   
                                                                          
  The impact you can now see

  ┌─────────────────────┬────────────────────┬───────────────────────┐
  │                     │    Sides wetted    │ Front only (sides in  │
  │                     │                    │         PTFE)         │
  ├─────────────────────┼────────────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ Power @ 30 l/min    │ ~17.5 W            │ ~2.9 W                │
  ├─────────────────────┼────────────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ Power @ 60 l/min    │ ~26 W (still       │ ~3.4 W → ceiling ~5 W │
  │                     │ climbing)          │                       │
  ├─────────────────────┼────────────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ Heater temp @ 30    │ ~83 °C             │ ~34 °C                │
  │ l/min               │                    │                       │
  ├─────────────────────┼────────────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ ±1σ flow res. @ 30  │ ~±0.26 l/min       │ ~±3.7 l/min           │
  │ l/min               │                    │                       │
  └─────────────────────┴────────────────────┴───────────────────────┘

  So insulating the sides makes it low-power, cool-running and trivially  
  feasible, but the 2 mm-buried NTC caps the power at ΔT/R_fn ≈ 5 W and 
  saturates the calibration at high flow — flow resolution degrades ~14×, 
  re-creating the single-probe weakness. The power chart visibly flattens
  and the King's-law √v fit no longer tracks it; the info line under the
  chart calls out the cap and the fix.

  The dominant lever is the NTC depth: drop NTC depth from face toward 0  
  and the cap lifts (R_fn → 0), recovering the non-saturating behaviour —
  confirming that the sensing point wants to sit on the wetted face, not  
  behind it. The README's element section documents the toggle and this
  trade.

⏺ Fixed — verified in the browser. The Gap: block→head control now works  
  in both wetting modes, adjustable from 0 (direct contact) outward, and  
  the diagram draws the block moving back with a labelled gap.            
                                                                          
  What it does now                                                        
                                                                          
  In insulated mode the heater→head conduction path is R_cond = (gap +
  back-to-NTC) / (k·A_head), so the gap is live:

  ┌───────────────────────────┬────────────────────────┬────────┐
  │ Gap (insulated) │  R_cond  │ Heater temp @ 30 l/min │ Power  │
  ├─────────────────┼──────────┼────────────────────────┼────────┤
  │ 0 (contact)     │ 1.4 K/W  │ 34 °C                  │ 2.93 W │
  ├─────────────────┼──────────┼────────────────────────┼────────┤
  │ 3 mm            │ 4.4 K/W  │ 43 °C                  │ 2.93 W │
  ├─────────────────┼──────────┼───────────────────────┼────────┤
  │ 10 mm           │ 11.4 K/W │ 64 °C                  │ 2.93 W │
  ├─────────────────┼──────────┼────────────────────────┼────────┤
  │ 30 mm           │ 31.5 K/W │ 122 °C                 │ 2.93 W │
  └─────────────────┴──────────┴────────────────────────┴────────┘

  One thing worth flagging

  The gap moves the required heater temperature (and eventually the       
  feasibility limit), but not the power/flow signal — the power stays 2.93
  W at every gap above. That's the correct physics: the gap sits upstream 
  of the held NTC, so with the NTC temperature pinned at ΔT, only what's
  between the NTC and the water (R_fn + convection) sets the heat
  throughput. Anything on the heater side of the NTC just changes how hot
  the heater has to run to push that fixed heat through.

  So the gap control is really a feasibility / heater-temperature lever   
  (how far back you can mount the block before the heater gets too hot),
  and the diagram + readout + the heater-temp feasibility flag all respond
  to it. If you'd expected it to change the power curve, that's why it
  doesn't — the lever for the power/saturation is the NTC depth (R_fn),
  not the block gap. The README notes both behaviours.

      127  sets the required heater temperature. The **bead** is st       
           ill the most power-efficient option; the                       
      128  **probe** is the chosen mechanical design and is now via

      129                                                                 
      130 -A **Head wetting** toggle (probe only) switches between        
          -*front + sides* wetted and *front face                  
      131 -only* (sides embedded in PTFE, block bonded to the back 
          -face). With the sides insulated, only the               
      132 -front disc exchanges heat **and** the buried NTC adds a 
          -series conduction resistance                            
      133 -`R_fn = ntc_depth / (k·A_head)` between it and the wette
          -d face. That **caps the power at                        
      134 -`ΔT/R_fn`** (~5 W with a 2 mm-deep NTC in 316) so the ca
          -libration **saturates at high flow** — the              
      135 -heater runs cool and efficient (~34 °C, ~3 W at 30 l/min
          -) but the flow resolution collapses                     
      136 -(~±0.26 → ~±3.7 l/min at 30 l/min). It re-creates the si
          -ngle-probe weakness; the cure is to put                 
      137 -the NTC right at the wetted face (`ntc_depth → 0`, which
          - lifts the cap). A clear illustration of                
      138 -why the sensing point wants to be on the wetted surface,
          - not buried behind it.                                  
      130 +A **Side wall wetted** control (probe only) sets how muc
          +h of the head's side wall is exposed to                 
      131 +the water, from 0 (front face only, sides in PTFE) up to
          + the full head thickness. It is the                     
      132 +direct lever on the **slope** of the power-vs-flow curve
          +, because the wetted area is                            
      133 +`A_wet = π/4·d² + π·d·L_side` and `P ≈ ΔT·h(v)·A_wet ∝ A
          +_wet·√v`. Sweeping the side exposure on

      141 +| 3.4 mm (full) | ~17.5 W | ~0.33 | none |              
      142 +                                                        
      143 +Two things move together as you expose more side: the **

          +nd finally disappears** at full exposure.

      147 +cool heater); at full exposure it is the strong non-satu
          +rating King's-law curve (best resolution,               
      148 +but more power and a hotter heater). The sensing point b
          +eing buried behind the wetted surface is                
      149 +what causes the saturation — full side wetting puts wett
          +ed metal right alongside the NTC.

          +set back by a neck) is adjustable too.

          +e NTC temperature held, only what lies                  
      154 +between the NTC and the water sets the heat throughput,

⏺ Done — verified in the browser. The binary toggle is replaced by a      
  continuous Side wall wetted slider (0 → full head thickness) in the     
  Probe controls.                                                         
                                                                          
  Your intuition was correct — and the tool now shows it                  
                                                                          
  The wetted area is A_wet = π/4·d² (front face) + π·d·L_side, and since P
  ≈ ΔT·h(v)·A_wet ∝ A_wet·√v, the side exposure directly sets the slope
  of power-vs-flow:

  ┌───────────────┬─────────────┬────────────────────┬──────────────┐
  │  Side wetted  │   P @ 30    │   slope (W per     │  high-flow   │
  │               │    l/min    │       l/min)       │     cap      │
  ├───────────────┼─────────────┼────────────────────┼──────────────┤
  │ 0 (front      │ 2.9 W       │ 0.02               │ ~5 W         │
  │ only)         │             │                    │              │
  ├───────────────┼─────────────┼────────────────────┼──────────────┤     
  │ 1 mm          │ 4.2 W       │ 0.03               │ ~7 W         │     
  ├───────────────┼─────────────┼────────────────────┼──────────────┤     
  │ 2 mm          │ 6.3 W       │ 0.06               │ ~12 W        │     
  ├───────────────┼─────────────┼───────────────────┼──────────────┤      
  │ 3.4 mm (full) │ 17.5 W      │ 0.33               │ none         │     
  └───────────────┴─────────────┴────────────────────┴──────────────┘     
                                                                          
  The slope climbs ~14× from front-only to fully wetted.                  
                                                                          
  Two effects move together (worth knowing)                               
                                                                          
  Exposing more side does two things at once:                             
  1. More wetted area → steeper slope (what you asked for).               
  2. The buried-NTC series resistance R_fn = (1 −                         
  L_side/thickness)·ntc_depth/(k·A) fades out, so the high-flow saturation
  cap (ΔT/R_fn) rises and finally disappears at full exposure.            
                                                                          
  So at low exposure the probe behaves like the old single probe (shallow 
  slope, saturating, but low power and a cool heater); at full exposure   
  it's the strong non-saturating King's-law curve (best resolution, but   
  ~17 W and a hotter heater). The diagram draws the PTFE collar over just 
  the un-wetted back portion of the head sides and labels the exposed     
  length, and the power/duty/accuracy charts all update live so you can   
  watch the slope and saturation change as you drag the slider.           
                                                                          
  The README's element section now documents this control with the sweep  
  table. Net design read: side exposure is your main knob for trading     
  flow-rate sensitivity against power draw / heater temperature.          
                                                                          
✻ Crunched for 8m 14s                                                     
                                                                          
❯ If the turn on transient goes to full power when delta T is 0 why does  
  the turn on transient for Air not rise fastest. Looks like the tun on   
  transient is being heated by the power required for each different      
  scenario, but that will only be known once the delta T is achieved.     
                                                                          
⏺ Good catch — that's a real bug. Let me look at the turn-on model:       
                                                                          
  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)                                          
                                                                          
⏺ Confirmed — the bug is Kp = p.loop_gain * G: the drive is scaled by each
  scenario's heat-loss conductance, so air (small G) gets little power    
  and rises slowly. The controller gain must be medium-independent so a   
  cold start saturates to P_max for every scenario. Let me fix it:        
                                                                          
⏺ Update(design/double-ntc-explorer/model.js)                             
  ⎿  Added 15 lines, removed 11 lines                                     
      477    // ---------------------------------------------------       
           -------------------------                                      
      478                                                                 
      479    /*                                                           
      480 -   * Turn-on: the element starts at T_fluid and the loop       
          - drives it (up to the rail                              
      481 -   * power P_max) toward the set-point T_fluid+ΔT. Lumpe
          -d explicit-Euler with a                                 
      482 -   * simple proportional+clamped controller, so each med
          -ium shows its settle time.                              
      483 -   * Returns {time:[s], T:[°C], P:[W], steadyP}.        
      480 +   * Turn-on: the element starts at T_fluid and the loop
          + drives it toward the                                   
      481 +   * set-point T_fluid+ΔT. The controller does NOT know 
          +the medium, so its gain is                              
      482 +   * medium-independent: a cold start (error = full ΔT) 
          +commands far more than the                              
      483 +   * rail can supply, so the drive clamps to P_max for E
          +VERY scenario. They therefore

  ⎿  cleaned                                                              
  ⎿  Allowed by auto mode classifier                                      
                                                                          
⏺ You were exactly right. The bug was in the controller gain.             
                                                                          
  The bug                                                                 
                                                                          
  const Kp = p.loop_gain * G;          // ← gain scaled by the medium's   
  heat-loss conductance                                                   
  let P = Kp * (setpoint - T);
  P = Math.min(Pmax, P);

  Because the proportional gain was multiplied by G (the scenario's       
  convective conductance), the commanded power was proportional to the
  heat loss of that medium — i.e. the element was being "heated by the    
  power required for each scenario," exactly as you said. Air has the
  smallest G, so it got the smallest drive and rose slowest. That's
  backwards, and it's also non-physical: the loop has no way to know G
  until ΔT is actually established.

  The fix

  The controller gain is now medium-independent:

  const Kp = (p.loop_gain * Pmax) / Math.max(0.1, p.delta_T);   // 
  independent of G                                                        
                                                                          
  On a cold start the error is the full ΔT, so Kp·ΔT = loop_gain·P_max —  
  far more than the rail can supply — and the drive clamps to P_max for   
  every scenario. So now:                                                 
                                                                          
  - All media start at 40 W and heat at the same initial rate P_max/C.    
  - The medium that bleeds off the least heat reaches ΔT first: air 0.85  
  s, still 0.85 s, flowing 1.12 s (probe) — air fastest, flowing slowest. 
  ✓                                                                       
  - A medium whose steady loss exceeds the rail (G·ΔT > P_max) now        
  correctly never reaches ΔT — it asymptotes below the set-point, which   
  also flags infeasibility in the transient.
                                                                          
  (I also fixed the explicit-Euler timestep to stay stable with the       
  now-high fixed gain.)
                                                                          
  The differences between media are modest when P_max ≫ the losses (the   
  heating is dominated by the rail and the thermal mass), which is the
  honest result — the earlier model exaggerated them for the wrong reason.
  The turn-on chart now shows the three curves rising together with air
  on top; lower Sim time to zoom in since the rise is sub-second for a
  small element. README updated.

✻ Cogitated for 4m 32s                                                    

❯ tell me what is in the PI loop and PWM driver, are those implemented in 
  the ESP32 or are they an external circuit or device ?                   
                                                                          
⏺ Short answer: as the schematic is drawn, the PI loop and the PWM driver 
  are an external analog circuit — not the ESP32. The ESP32 only watches  
  the result. That's the architecture we landed on earlier (the "pure     
  analog loop" discussion): the analog loop holds ΔT continuously, and the
  MCU just reads it.                                                      
                                                                          
  Here's what's actually in each block.

  PI loop (analog)

  A small op-amp circuit that turns the bridge imbalance into a
  heater-drive level:
  - Instrumentation / difference amp — takes V_A − V_B from the sensing
  bridge (the ΔT error).                                                  
  - Op-amp integrator — an op-amp with a feedback capacitor (+ a resistor
  for the proportional term = PI). The capacitor is the bit that holds a  
  steady output at zero error, so the heater keeps getting power once ΔT  
  is reached. The RC sets the loop bandwidth — that's what the tool's     
  loop_gain abstracts.                                                    
  - Parts: 1–2 op-amps, a few resistors, one capacitor.                   

  PWM driver (analog)                                                     
                                                                          
  Turns that control voltage into a switched heater drive:                
  - Oscillator — a sawtooth/triangle generator (a 555, or an op-amp       
  relaxation oscillator), fixed frequency.                                
  - Comparator — compares the PI control voltage against the sawtooth → a 
  square wave whose duty cycle follows the control voltage.               
  - Gate drive + power MOSFET (M1) — switches the 12 V rail into the 40 W 
  heater. (A linear pass-FET would also work but wastes power, as we      
  noted.)                                                                 
  - Parts: oscillator, comparator, gate resistor, the power MOSFET.       
                                                                          
  So the whole ΔT-holding loop is analog and runs on its own — no firmware
  in the fast path, and it keeps regulating even if the MCU is busy or    
  asleep.                                                                 
                                                                          
  What the ESP32 does (monitor only, in this design)                      

  - Reads the averaged current-sense voltage on its ADC → P =             
  I_avg·V_drive → flow_lpm(P) and classify() (air/still/flow) — exactly
  the C reference the tool emits.                                         
  - Reads the upstream NTC divider → fluid temperature (for display /
  compensation).
  - Drives the alarm/indicator and BLE.
  - It does not close the temperature loop or generate the heater PWM.    
                                                                          
  The alternative (and why it matters)                                    
                                                                          
  You could instead do the loop digitally in the ESP32: read both NTC     
  voltages on the ADC, compute the error in firmware, run a software PI,
  and output hardware PWM (the ESP32's LEDC peripheral) to the MOSFET     
  gate. Trade-offs:                                                       

  ┌────────┬───────────────────────────┬──────────────────────────────┐   
  │        │  Analog loop (as drawn)   │     Digital loop (ESP32)     │   
  ├────────┼───────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┤   
  │ ΔT set │ fixed resistor ratio (±10 │ firmware — exact,            │   
  │  by    │  % drift)                 │ programmable, can linearise  │   
  │        │                           │ the NTC                      │   
  ├────────┼───────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┤   
  │        │ continuous, fast,         │ limited by ADC sampling +    │   
  │ Speed  │ MCU-independent           │ loop rate; MCU must stay     │   
  │        │                           │ awake                        │
  ├────────┼───────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┤   
  │ Parts  │ more analog (op-amps,     │ fewer analog parts; relies   │   
  │        │ oscillator, comparator)   │ on the noisy ESP32 ADC       │
  └────────┴───────────────────────────┴──────────────────────────────┘   
                                                                          
  The tool itself is neutral on this — it models the loop as an idealized 
  loop_gain plus the current-sense ADC chain, so either implementation
  realizes the same physics. The schematic just depicts the analog        
  version, and the emitted C code is the monitor-side firmware for it.

  If it'd help, I can annotate the schematic with a dashed boundary       
  showing what's external analog vs. what's inside the ESP32 — or sketch
  the digital-loop variant (ADC×2 → firmware PI → LEDC PWM → MOSFET) as a 
  second diagram. Want either?

⏺ Done. I created a runnable LTspice netlist at                           
  design/double-ntc-explorer/ltspice/doublentc_ctd_loop.cir and verified
  its behaviour numerically before handing it over.                       
                                                                          
  What it is                                                              
                                                                          
  A behavioural electrothermal model of the analog control loop — it lets 
  you watch the loop actually regulate, not just the electronics in
  isolation. It uses the standard thermal↔electrical analogy so one       
  simulation captures both domains:                                       

  ┌────────────────────┬──────────────────────┐
  │      Thermal       │      Electrical      │
  ├────────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ temperature (°C)   │ node voltage (V)     │
  ├────────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ heat flow (W)      │ B-source current (A) │
  ├────────────────────┼─────────────────────┤
  │ thermal R (K/W)    │ resistance (Ω)       │
  ├────────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ thermal mass (J/K) │ capacitance (F)      │
  └────────────────────┴──────────────────────┘

  The netlist has the same chain as the README schematic — difference amp
  → PI integrator (with anti-windup) → sawtooth + comparator → PWM'd
  MOSFET → heater — driving a one-node thermal plant (element mass +
  convective loss to the fluid). All built from behavioural B-sources, so
  it opens and runs in any LTspice with no external models/libraries.

  How to run it

  Open the .cir in LTspice (File → Open) and click Run. Plot:
  - V(Tdn) — element temperature: climbs from 20 and holds at fluid + ΔT
  (= 30).                                                                 
  - V(Pavg) — delivered heater power: settles at G·ΔT.
  - V(ctrl)/V(gate) — the PWM duty demand and the switching waveform.     
                                                                          
  A flow step at t = 3 s (G: 1.0 → 1.746 W/K) is built in, so you can
  watch the loop raise the power to keep Tdn pinned.                      
                                                                          
  Verified                                                                
                                                                          
  I ran the same equations/gains in Python: the loop is stable, holds Tdn 
  steps power ~10 W → ~17.5 W = G·ΔT at the flow step — exactly the       
  constant-ΔT behaviour. Kp, Ki, ΔT, Pmax, flow Gf, and the PWM frequency 
  are all .params at the top to tune, and there are commented alternatives
  (skip-PWM fast check, fixed-flow calibration, change fluid temp for the 
  compensation check).                                                    
                                                                          
  Two honest notes: it models the loop/system dynamics with the NTC bridge
  represented directly by temperature nodes (not component-level dividers 
  with real op-amp models) — that's the right level to verify the loop    
  holds ΔT and the PWM averages correctly. If you want a component-level  
  version next (real op-amp models like UniversalOpamp2, an NTC Beta-model
  bridge, a real MOSFET, a 555 oscillator) to verify the actual           
  breadboard circuit, I can build that as a follow-up — it's more faithful
  to the hardware but needs LTspice's bundled libraries.
